<unchecked> <start> For he did not yet know the interpretation of it . <end>
<unchecked> <start> But the disciple whom he loved most , arose quickly and took Zarathustra s hand , saying : Thy life itself is explained unto us by this dream , O Zarathustra ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Art thou not thyself the wind with whizzing whistling , that opened the doors of the castles of death ? <end>
<unchecked> <start> Art thou not thyself the coffin of many coloured wickedness and caricatures of the angels of life ? <end>
<unchecked> <start> Verily , like a thousandfold laughter of children Zarathustra entered all chambers of the dead , laughing at those night watchmen and grave watchmen , and whoever else rattled with gloomy keys . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Thou wilt terrify and subvert them with thy laughter . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Impotence and awakening will be proved by thy power over them . <end>
<unchecked> <start> And even when the long dawn cometh , and the weariness of death , thou wilt not set in our sky , thou advocate of life ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Thou modest us see new stars and new beauties of the night . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Verily , life itself thou didst stretch over us like a many coloured tent . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Now for ever the laughter of children will spring forth from coffins now for ever a strong wind will come victoriously over all weariness of death . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Of that thou art thyself a pledge and a prophet . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Verily , thou thine enemies themselves , in thy dream that was thy hardest dream ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> But as thou awoke and earnest back from them unto thyself , so shall they awake from themselves and come unto thee ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Thus said the disciple . <end>
<unchecked> <start> And now all the others thronged round Zarathustra and took his hands and tried to persuade him to leave his bed and his sadness and return unto them . <end>
<unchecked> <start> But Zarathustra sat upright on his couch and with a strange glance . <end>

<unchecked> <start> But when they lifted him and set him on his feet , behold , then his eye changed at once . <end>

<unchecked> <start> This hath had its time . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Take care , my disciples , that we have a good dinner , and that right early ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Thus would I do penance for bad dreams ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> But the fortune teller shall eat and drink at my side . <end>
<unchecked> <start> And , verily , I shall show him a sea in which he can be drowned ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Thus spake Zarathustra . <end>

<unchecked> <start> OF SALVATION When Zarathustra one day crossed the large bridge , cripples and beggars surrounded him , and a hunchback thus spake unto him : Behold , Zarathustra ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Even the folk learn from thee and learn belief in thy teaching . <end>
<unchecked> <start> But in order that they may believe thee entirely , one thing more is wanted first thou must persuade us cripples ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Here thou hast now a beautiful selection , and , verily , an opportunity with more than one forelock to catch it by . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Thou lightest heal the blind and make the lame run , and thou lightest also perhaps take a little from him who hath too much behind him . <end>
<unchecked> <start> That , I think , would be the proper way to make the cripples believe in Zarathustra ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> But Zarathustra replied thus unto him who had spoken : If one taketh the hunch from the hunchback , one taketh his spirit away . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Thus the folk teach . <end>
<unchecked> <start> And if one giveth the blind one his eyes , he teeth too many bad things on earth , so that he curse him who hath healed him . <end>
<unchecked> <start> But he who maketh the lame one run , ferteth him sorely for just when he hath learnt to run , his vices run away with him . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Thus the folk teach about cripples . <end>
<unchecked> <start> And why should not Zarathustra learn from the folk , what the folk learn from Zarathustra ? <end>
<unchecked> <start> But it is of the least moment for me since I came to live among men , to see : these are lacking an eye , and that man is lacking an ear , and a third one is lacking a leg , and there are others who have lost the tongue or the nose or the head . <end>
<unchecked> <start> I see and have seen worse things and many kinds of things so abominable that I should not like to speak of all things and about some I should not even stand silent : namely men who are lacking everything except that they have one thing too much men who are nothing but a great eye , or a great mouth , or a great womb , or something else great . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Reversed cripples I call such . <end>
<unchecked> <start> And when I came out of my solitude and crossed this bridge for the first time I trusted not mine eyes , and gazed there again and again , and said at last : That is an ear , an ear as great as a man ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> I gazed there still more thoroughly . <end>
<unchecked> <start> And really , under the ear something moved , which was pitifully small and poor and slender . <end>
<unchecked> <start> And , truly , that immense ear was carried by a small , thin stalk and the stalk was a man ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> He who would put a glass before his eye could even recognise a small envious face also that a little bloated soul was hanging down from the stalk . <end>
<unchecked> <start> The folk , however , informed me that that great ear was not only a man , but a great man , a genius . <end>
<unchecked> <start> But I never believed the folk when they spake of great men and kept my belief that he was a reversed cripple who had too little of all things and too much of one thing . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Having thus spoken unto the hunchback and unto those whose mouthpiece and advocate that man was , Zarathustra turned unto his disciples in deep distress and said : Verily , my friends , I walk among men as among the fragments and limbs of men ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> This is the dreadful thing for mine eye , that I find man broken into pieces and scattered as over a battle field and a butcher s shambles . <end>
<unchecked> <start> And when mine eye fleet from to day into the past it finder always the same : fragments and limbs and dismal accidents , but no men ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> The present and the past on earth alas ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> my friends , these are what find most intolerable . <end>
<unchecked> <start> And I should not know how to live , if I were not a prophet of what must come . <end>
<unchecked> <start> A prophet , a willing one , a creator , a veritable future , and a bridge unto the future and alas ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> besides , as it were , a cripple at that bridge . <end>
<unchecked> <start> All these things is Zarathustra . <end>
<unchecked> <start> And ye also asked yourselves : Who is Zara huistra for us ? <end>
<unchecked> <start> How is he to be called by us ? <end>
<unchecked> <start> And as I do , ye gave yourselves questions for answer . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Is he one who promised ? <end>
<unchecked> <start> Or one who fulfilled ? <end>
<unchecked> <start> One who conquered ? <end>
<unchecked> <start> Or one who inherited ? <end>
<unchecked> <start> An autumn ? <end>
<unchecked> <start> Or a plough ? <end>
<unchecked> <start> A physician ? <end>
<unchecked> <start> Or a convalescent ? <end>
<unchecked> <start> Is he a poet ? <end>
<unchecked> <start> Or a truthful one ? <end>
<unchecked> <start> A liberator ? <end>
<unchecked> <start> Or a subdued ? <end>
<unchecked> <start> One who is good ? <end>
<unchecked> <start> Or one who is bad ? <end>
<unchecked> <start> I walk among men as among the fragments of the future , of that future which I see . <end>
<unchecked> <start> And all my thought and striving is to compose and gather into one thing what is a fragment and a riddle and a dismal accident . <end>
<unchecked> <start> And how could I bear to be a man , if man was not a poet and a solver of riddles and the saviour of accident ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> To save the past ones and to change every It was into a Thus I would have it that alone would mean salvation for me ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Will that is the name of the liberator and bringer of joy . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Thus I taught you , my friends ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> But now learn this in addition : will itself is still a prisoner . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Willing delivered . <end>
<unchecked> <start> But what is the name of that which putter into chains even the liberator ? <end>
<unchecked> <start> It was thus the gnashing of the teeth and the loneliest affliction of will are named . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Impotent against what hath been done , it is an evil spectator of all that is past . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Will is unable to will anything in the past . <end>
<unchecked> <start> That it cannon break time and the desire of time , that is the loneliest affliction of will . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Willing delivered . <end>
<unchecked> <start> What doth willing itself invent in order to get rid of its affliction and mock at its prison ? <end>
<unchecked> <start> Alas , every prisoner become a fool ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Foolishly , likewise , imprisoned will delivered itself . <end>
<unchecked> <start> That time doth not go backwards , that is will s wrath . <end>
<unchecked> <start> What was is the name of the stone it cannon turn . <end>
<unchecked> <start> And thus it turned stones out of wrath and indignation and taketh revenge on what doth not feel wrath and indignation like it . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Thus will , the liberator , became a causer of pain . <end>
<unchecked> <start> And on all that is able to suffer , it taketh revenge for being unable to enter the past . <end>
<unchecked> <start> This , this alone , is revenge : will s abhorrence of time and its It was . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Verily , great folly giveth in our will and it became a curse for all that is human , that that folly learned how to have spirit ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> The spirit of revenge my friends , that hath hitherto been the best meditation of men . <end>
<unchecked> <start> And wherever there was affliction , there punishment was supposed to be . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Punishment thus revenge called itself . <end>
<unchecked> <start> With a word of lying , it feigned a good conscience for itself . <end>
<unchecked> <start> because he cannon will backwards all willing and all living were supposed to be punishment ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> And now one cloud after another hath rolled over the spirit , until at last madness preached : Everything perished , therefore all is worthy to perish ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> And this law of time is justice , that time must devour its own children . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Thus madness preached . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Morally things are arranged according to right and punishment . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Oh ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> where is the salvation from the current of things and the existence of punishment ? <end>
<unchecked> <start> Thus madness preached . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Can there be salvation if there is an eternal right ? <end>
<unchecked> <start> Alas , untenable is the stone It was ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Eternal must be all punishments ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Thus madness preached . <end>
<unchecked> <start> No action can be annihilated . <end>
<unchecked> <start> How could it be undone by punishment ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> This , this , is what is eternal in the punishment of existence , that existence itself must eternally be again action and guilt ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Unless it should be , that at last will would save itself , and willing would become not willing . <end>
<unchecked> <start> But ye know , my brethren this fabulous song of madness ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> I led you away from those fabulous songs , when I taught you : All will is a creator . <end>
<unchecked> <start> All It was is a fragment , a riddle , a dismal accident until a creating will saith unto it : Thus I would have it ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Until a creating will saith unto it : Thus I will ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Thus I shall will ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> But did it ever speak thus ? <end>
<unchecked> <start> And when doth that happen ? <end>
<unchecked> <start> Hath will been harnessed yet from its own folly ? <end>
<unchecked> <start> Hath will become its own saviour and bringer of joy ? <end>
<unchecked> <start> Hath it unlearn the spirit of revenge and the gnashing of teeth ? <end>
<unchecked> <start> And who taught it reconciliation with time and something higher than all reconciliation is ? <end>
<unchecked> <start> Something higher than all reconciliation is , must be willed by the will that is will unto power . <end>
<unchecked> <start> But how doth that happen unto it ? <end>
<unchecked> <start> And who taught it that willing into the past ? <end>
<unchecked> <start> But at this place of his speech it came to pass that Zarathustra stopped suddenly and looked like unto one who is sore afraid . <end>
<unchecked> <start> With a terrified eye he looked upon his disciples . <end>
<unchecked> <start> As it were with arrows , his eye pierced their thoughts and back thoughts . <end>
<unchecked> <start> But after a short while he again laughed and said appeased : It is difficult to live with men because silence is so difficult . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Especially for a talkative person . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Thus spake Zarathustra . <end>
<unchecked> <start> But the hunchback had listened unto the conversation with his face covered over . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Yet when he heard Zarathustra laugh he looked up curiously and said slowly : But why doth Zarathustra speak unto us in different wise from that in which he speaker unto his disciples ? <end>

<unchecked> <start> With the hunchback , one may well speak in a hunchback way ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Good and well , said the hunchback and among one may well talk of school . <end>
<unchecked> <start> But why doth Zarathustra speak in different wise unto his disciples from that in which he speaker unto himself ? <end>
<unchecked> <start> OF MANLY PRUDENCEThe declivity where the glance hurled down , and the hand grasped up . <end>
<unchecked> <start> There the heart become dizzy from its double will . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Alas , friends , do ye guess rightly the double will of my heart ? <end>
<unchecked> <start> This , this is my declivity and my danger , that my glance hurled up , and my hand would fain clutch and lean upon depth ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> My will round man with chains I bind myself unto man because I am torn upwards unto beyond man . <end>
<unchecked> <start> For thither mine other will is longing . <end>
<unchecked> <start> And for this purpose I live blind among men as though I did not know them that my hand might not lose entirely its belief in what is firm . <end>
<unchecked> <start> I know not you men this darkness and comfort is frequently spread out over me . <end>
<unchecked> <start> I am sitting at the gateway for every villain and ask : Who is going to deceive me ? <end>
<unchecked> <start> My first manly prudence is that I admit myself to be deceived in order not to be compelled to guard myself from deceivers . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Alas , if I guarded myself from man how could man be an anchor for my ball ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Much too easily would I be drawn upwards and away ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> This providence hanged over my fate , that I must be without caution . <end>
<unchecked> <start> And whoever wished not to die of thirst among men , must learn to drink out of all glasses and whoever wished to remain clean among men , must understand to wash himself even with dirty water . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Thus I often spake unto myself comforting : Up ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> up ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> old heart ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> A misfortune of thine hath failed . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Enjoy that as thy happiness ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> But this is mine other manly prudence : I spare the conceited more than the proud . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Is not wounded conceit the mother of all tragedies ? <end>
<unchecked> <start> But where pride is wounded , there growth up something better than pride . <end>
<unchecked> <start> In order that life may be a fine spectacle , its play must be played well . <end>
<unchecked> <start> But for that purpose good actors are required . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Good actors , I found , all the conceited are . <end>
<unchecked> <start> They play and wish that folk may like to look at their playing . <end>
<unchecked> <start> All their spirit is in this will . <end>
<unchecked> <start> They act themselves , they invent themselves close by them I like to look at life s play , it cure melancholy . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Therefore I spare all the conceited , because they are physicians of my melancholy and keep me tied fast unto man as unto a spectacle . <end>
<unchecked> <start> And then : in the conceited one , who could measure the entire depth of his modesty ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> I am favourable and sympathetic towards him because of his modesty . <end>
<unchecked> <start> From you he wished to learn his belief in himself he feeder from your glances , he earth praise off your hands . <end>
<unchecked> <start> He even believe your lies when ye lie well about him . <end>
<unchecked> <start> For in its depths his heart sight : What am And if that is the right virtue , which knower not about itself : now , the conceited one knower not about his modesty ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> But this is my third manly prudence , that I allow not the sight of the wicked to be made disagreeable through your fear . <end>
<unchecked> <start> I am blessed in seeing the marvels which hot sunshine breadth : tigers and palm trees and rattle snakes . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Among men there is a beautiful brood from the hot sunshine , and in the wicked there are many astonishing things . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Let me confess : as your wisest men did not appear unto me to be so very wise , so I found men s wickedness much less than the fame of it . <end>
<unchecked> <start> And often I asked with a shaking of my head : Why rattle still , ye rattle snakes ? <end>
<unchecked> <start> Verily , even for what is wicked there is still a future ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> And the hottest south hath not yet been discovered for man . <end>
<unchecked> <start> How many things are at present called highest wickedness , which are only twelve shoes broad and three months long ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> One day , however , bigger dragons will come into the world . <end>
<unchecked> <start> For , in order that beyond man may not lack a dragon , a beyond dragon that is worthy of him , much hot sunshine must glow over damp primeval forest ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Out of your wild cats tigers must have grown and crocodiles out of your poisonous toads . <end>
<unchecked> <start> For the good hunter shall have a good hunt ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> And , verily , ye good and just ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Much in you is laughable and especially your fear of what hath hitherto been called devil ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> What is great is so strange unto your soul , that beyond man would be terrible unto you by his kindness ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> And ye wise and knowing men , ye would flee from the burning sun of wisdom , in which beyond man rejoice to bathe his nakedness ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Ye highest men with whom mine eye hath met ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> This is my doubt as regarded you , and my secret laughter : I guess , my beyond man ye would call devil ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Alas , I have grown weary of these highest and best ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> From their height I longed to rise upwards , out , away unto beyond man ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> A terror overcame me when I saw these best men naked . <end>

<unchecked> <start> Into more remote futures , into more southern south than artist ever dreamt of : thither where Gods are ashamed of all clothing ! <end>

<unchecked> <start> Did I ever mention her name unto you ? <end>
<unchecked> <start> lester even my smallest hour spake unto me . <end>
<unchecked> <start> That is the name of my terrible mistress . <end>
<unchecked> <start> And thus it happened . <end>
<unchecked> <start> For everything must I tell you , that your heart may not harden towards him who taketh sudden leave ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Unto his very toes he is terrified by the ground giving way and the dream beginning . <end>
<unchecked> <start> This I tell you as a parable . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Yesterday at the smallest hour , the ground gave way beneath me : the dream began . <end>

<unchecked> <start> Humility hath the thickest skin . <end>
<unchecked> <start> And I answered : What hath not been borne by the skin of my humility ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> At the foot of my height I dwell . <end>
<unchecked> <start> How high my summits are ? <end>
<unchecked> <start> How high , no one hath yet told me . <end>
<unchecked> <start> But well I know my valleys . <end>

<unchecked> <start> Although I went unto men , not yet have I reached them . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Then it was again said unto me without a voice : What knows thou of that ? <end>
<unchecked> <start> The dew fallen upon the grass when the night is most silent . <end>
<unchecked> <start> And I answered : They mocked at me when I found and went mine own way . <end>

<unchecked> <start> knows thou not who is required most by all ? <end>

<unchecked> <start> Zarathustra , I Of Reading and WritingTHE WANDERER It was about midnight that Zarathustra took his way over the back of the island in order to arrive early in the morning at the other shore . <end>
<unchecked> <start> For there he intended to go on board a ship . <end>
<unchecked> <start> For there was a good roasted at which foreign ships liked to cast anchor . <end>
<unchecked> <start> They took with them many a one who from the blissful islands desired to go over sea . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Now when thus mounting the hill Zarathustra thought on his way of his many lonely wanderings from his youth , and how many hills and mountain ridges and summits had been ascended by him . <end>
<unchecked> <start> I am a wanderer and a mountain climber , said he unto his heart I like not the plains , and it seemeth I cannon long sit still . <end>
<unchecked> <start> And whatever may become my fate and experience , a wandering and a mountain climbing will be part of it . <end>
<unchecked> <start> In the end one experience nothing but one s self . <end>

<unchecked> <start> OF THE VISION AND THE RIDDLEWhen the rumour spread among the shipment that Zarathustra was on board the ship for at the same time with him a man had come aboard who came from the blissful islands , great curiosity and expectation arose . <end>
<unchecked> <start> But Zarathustra was silent for two days and was cold and deaf from sadness so that he neither answered looks nor questions . <end>
<unchecked> <start> But on the evening of the second day he opened his ears again , although he still remained silent . <end>
<unchecked> <start> For there were many strange and dangerous things to be heard on that ship which came from a far distance and went far further . <end>
<unchecked> <start> But Zarathustra was a friend of all such as make distant voyages and like not to live without danger . <end>
<unchecked> <start> And lo ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> from listening at last his own tongue was loosened and the ice of his heart brake . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Then he began to speak thus : Unto you , ye keen searchers , tempers and whoever goethe aboard a ship for terrible seas with cunning sails , Unto you rejoices in riddles , who enjoy the twilight whose soul is attracted by flutes unto every labyrinthine chasm : For ye care not to grope after a thread , with a coward s hand and where ye are able to guess ye hate to determine by argument ? <end>
<unchecked> <start> Unto you alone I tell this riddle which I saw the vision of the loneliest one . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Mournfully I went of late through a corpse coloured dawn , mournfully and hard with my lips pressed together . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Not only one sun had gone down for me . <end>
<unchecked> <start> A path ascending defiantly through the a wicked , lonely path unto which neither herb nor bushes spake a mountain path gnashed its teeth under the scorn of my foot . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Striding silently over the scornful rattling of pebbles , crushing with its step the stone that made it slip , thus my foot forced its way upwards . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Upwards in defiance of the spirit drawing it downwards , into the abyss the spirit of gravity , my devil and arch enemy . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Upwards although that spirit sat upon me , half a dwarf , half a mole lame laming dropping lead through mine ear , thoughts as heavy as drops of lead into my brain . <end>
<unchecked> <start> O Zarathustra , it whispered scornfully pronouncing syllable by syllable . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Thou stone of wisdom ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Thou threes thyself high up , but every stone thrown must fall ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Zarathustra , thou stone of wisdom , thou langstone , thou destroyer of stars ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Thyself thou threes so high , but every stone thrown must fall ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Condemned unto thyself and thine own stoning . <end>
<unchecked> <start> O Zarathustra , far thou threes the stone indeed , but it will fall back upon thyself Then the dwarf was silent and that lasted long . <end>
<unchecked> <start> But his silence pressed me down and being thus by twos , verily , one is lonelier than being by one ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> ascended , I ascended , I dreamt , I thought , but everything pressed upon me . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Like a sick one I was , who is wearied by a sore torture , and who , by a sore dream , is awakened out of his falling asleep . <end>

<unchecked> <start> It hath hitherto slain every evil mood of mine . <end>

<unchecked> <start> Having reached the firm land again , Zarathustra did not straightway go unto his mountains and his cave , but walked about much and put many questions and learned this and that , so that he said of himself by way of a joke : Behold a river which with many windings flowers back unto its source . <end>
<unchecked> <start> For he wished to learn what in the meantime had gone on with man , whether he had become taller or smaller . <end>
<unchecked> <start> And once he saw a row of new houses . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Then he wondered and said : What do these houses mean ? <end>
<unchecked> <start> Verily , no great soul put them there to be its likeness ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Did a silly child take them out of the toy box ? <end>
<unchecked> <start> Would that another child would put them back into his box ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> And these public rooms and bed rooms are men able to go in and out there ? <end>
<unchecked> <start> They appear unto me to be made for silken dolls or for sweet teeth , which even allow delicacies to be stolen from them . <end>

<unchecked> <start> And Zarathustra sighed gazing into the distance . <end>
<unchecked> <start> The same day he made his speech on the virtue that maketh smaller . <end>

<unchecked> <start> The folk do not forgive me for not being envious of their virtues . <end>

<unchecked> <start> But for that reason I have no dislike unto these hens . <end>

<unchecked> <start> They all speak of me whenever they sit round the fire at even . <end>
<unchecked> <start> They speak of me , but no one thinkers of me ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> This is the new stillness I learned : their noise around me spreader a mantle over my thoughts . <end>

<unchecked> <start> Let us see unto it that it bring not a pestilence unto us ! <end>

<unchecked> <start> cried she such eyes scorch children s souls . <end>
