<unchecked> <start> They do not divine anything about the rushing of my happiness ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> We have not yet time for Zarathustra they say as an objection . <end>
<unchecked> <start> But what matter about a time that hath no time for Zarathustra ? <end>
<unchecked> <start> And if they praise me , above all , how can I fall asleep on their fame ? <end>
<unchecked> <start> A belt of spikes is their praising unto me it scratched me even when I take it off . <end>
<unchecked> <start> And this moreover I learned among them : the praising one behave as if he restored things in truth , however , he desire to be given more ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Ask my foot whether it is pleased by their melody of praising and alluring ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Verily , unto such a time beat and ticking it lieth neither to dance nor to stand still . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Unto small virtue they would fain allure me and draw me by praising . <end>
<unchecked> <start> To share the ticking of their small happiness , they would fain persuade my foot . <end>
<unchecked> <start> I walk through these folk and keep mine eyes open . <end>
<unchecked> <start> They have become smaller and are becoming ever smaller . <end>
<unchecked> <start> And the reason of that is their doctrine of happiness and virtue . <end>
<unchecked> <start> For they are modest even in their virtue for they are desirous of ease . <end>
<unchecked> <start> But with ease only modest virtue is compatible . <end>
<unchecked> <start> True , in their fashion they learn how to stride and to stride forward . <end>
<unchecked> <start> That call I their hobbling . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Thereby they become an offence unto every one who is in a hurry . <end>
<unchecked> <start> And many a one stride on and in doing so looked backward , with a stiffened neck . <end>
<unchecked> <start> I rejoice to run against the stomachs of such . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Foot and eyes shall not lie , nor reproach each other for lying . <end>
<unchecked> <start> But there is much lying among the small folk . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Some of them will , but most of them are willed merely . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Some of them are genuine , but most of them are bad actors . <end>
<unchecked> <start> There are unconscious actors among them , and involuntary actors . <end>
<unchecked> <start> The genuine are always rare , especially genuine actors . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Here is little of man therefore women try to make themselves manly . <end>
<unchecked> <start> For only he who is enough of a man will save the woman in woman . <end>
<unchecked> <start> And this hypocrisy I found to be worst among them , that even those who command feign the virtues of those who serve . <end>
<unchecked> <start> I serve , thou serves , we serve . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Thus the hypocrisy of the rulers prayer . <end>
<unchecked> <start> And , alas , if the highest lord be merely the highest servant ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Alas ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> the curiosity of mine eye strayed even unto their hypocrisies , and well I divined all their fly happiness and their humming round window panes in the sunshine . <end>
<unchecked> <start> So much kindness , so much weakness see I . <end>
<unchecked> <start> So much justice and sympathy , so much weakness . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Round , honest and kind are they towards each other , as grains of sand are round , honest and kind unto grains of sand . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Modestly to embrace a small happiness they call submission ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> And therewith they modestly look sideways after a new small happiness . <end>
<unchecked> <start> At bottom they desire plainly one thing most of all : to be hurt by nobody . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Thus they oblige all and do well unto them . <end>
<unchecked> <start> But this is cowardice although it be called virtue . <end>
<unchecked> <start> And if once they speak harshly , these small folk , I hear therein merely their hoarseness . <end>
<unchecked> <start> For every draught of air maketh them hoarse . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Prudent are they their virtues have prudent fingers . <end>
<unchecked> <start> But they are lacking in clenched fists their fingers know not how to hide themselves behind fists . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Thereby they have made the wolf a dog and man himself man s best domestic animal . <end>
<unchecked> <start> We put our chair in the midst thus saith their simpering unto me exactly as far from dying gladiators as from happy swine . <end>
<unchecked> <start> This is mediocrity although it be called moderation . <end>
<unchecked> <start> I walk through these folk and let fall many a word . <end>
<unchecked> <start> But they know neither how to take nor how to keep . <end>
<unchecked> <start> They wonder that I have not come to revile lusts and vices . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Nor indeed have I come to bid them beware of pick pockets . <end>
<unchecked> <start> They wonder that I am not ready to sharpen and point their prudence as if among them there were not hirelings enough , whose voices grate mine ear like slate pencils . <end>
<unchecked> <start> And when I cry : Curse all cowardly devils within yourselves who would fain whine and fold their hands and adore they cry : Zarathustra is ungodly . <end>
<unchecked> <start> And so chiefly their teachers of submission cry . <end>
<unchecked> <start> But into their ears I rejoice to cry : Yea ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> I am Zarathustra , the ungodly ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> These teachers of submission ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Like lice they creep wherever things are small and sick and stabbed . <end>
<unchecked> <start> It is only my loathing that hindered me from cracking them . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Up ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> This is my sermon unto their ears : I am Zarathustra , the ungodly , who ask : Who is more ungodly than I am that I may enjoy his teaching ? <end>
<unchecked> <start> I am Zarathustra , the ungodly . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Where find I my like ? <end>
<unchecked> <start> And all those are my like who give themselves a will of their own and renounce all submission . <end>
<unchecked> <start> I am Zarathustra , the ungodly . <end>
<unchecked> <start> I have ever boiled every chance in mine own pot . <end>
<unchecked> <start> And not until it hath been boiled properly , do I give it welcome as my meat . <end>
<unchecked> <start> And , verily , many a chance came unto me imperiously . <end>
<unchecked> <start> But my will spake unto it still more so . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Then the chance at once fell beseechingly upon its knees Beseeching to be given a home and heart with me , and persuading me flatteringly : Behold , O Zarathustra , how ever friend cometh unto friend ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> But what say I where no one hath mine ears ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> And thus I will proclaim it into all winds : Ye become ever smaller , ye small folk ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Ye comfortable ones , ye crumble away ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> One day ye will perish From your many small virtues , from your many small omissions , from your much small submission ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Too much sparing , too much yielding thus it is your soil ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> But for the purpose of growing high a tree will twist hard roots round hard rocks ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Even what ye omit wealth at the weft of all manly future even your nothing is a spider s web and a spider living upon the blood of the future . <end>
<unchecked> <start> And when ye take anything , it is as if ye stole it , ye small virtuous . <end>
<unchecked> <start> But even among rogues honour ordered : One shall steal only when one cannon rob . <end>
<unchecked> <start> It is given that is one of those doctrines of submission . <end>
<unchecked> <start> But I tell you , ye comfortable ones : It is taken and ever more will be taken from you ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Oh , that ye would renounce that half willing and resolve upon idleness as one resolved upon action ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Oh , that ye would understand my word : Be sure to do whatever ye like , but first of all be such as can will ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Be sure to love your neighbour as yourselves , but first of all be such as love themselves As love themselves with great love , with great contempt ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Thus speaker Zarathustra , the ungodly . <end>
<unchecked> <start> But what say I where no one hath mine ears ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Here it is still an hour too early for me . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Mine own fore runner I am among these folk , mine own cock crow through dark lanes . <end>
<unchecked> <start> But their hour will come ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> And mine will come also ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Every hour they become smaller , poorer , less fertile . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Poor pot herbs ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Poor soil ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> And soon shall they stand there like dry grass and prairie , and , verily , wearied of themselves and longing for fire more than for water ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Oh , blessed hour of lightning ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Oh , secret of the forenoon ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Running fires shall I one day make out of them and announcers with fiery tongues . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Announce shall they one day with fiery tongues : It cometh , it is nigh , the great noon ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> The winter , an evil guest , sitter in my home with me . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Blue are my hands from his friendship s handshaking . <end>
<unchecked> <start> I honour him , this evil guest , but would gladly let him alone . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Gladly I run away from him . <end>
<unchecked> <start> And , if one kenneth well one escape from him ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> With warm feet and warm thoughts I run thither where the wind is still , unto the sunny corner of my mount of olives . <end>
<unchecked> <start> There I laugh at my stern guest and yet am fond of him , because at home he catches the flies for me and stilled many little noises . <end>
<unchecked> <start> For he doth not allow a midge to sing , or still less two midges even the lane he maketh lonely so that the moonshine at night is afraid there . <end>
<unchecked> <start> A hard guest is he , but I honour him , and I do not , like the tendering , pray unto the fire idol with its fat womb . <end>
<unchecked> <start> idols ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Thus my kin willett . <end>
<unchecked> <start> And especially I hate all ardent , steaming , damp fire idols . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Whom I love , I love better in winter than in summer . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Better I now mock at mine enemies , and more valiantly , now that the winter sitter in my home . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Valiantly indeed , even when I creep into bed . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Even then my hidden happiness laughed and then laughed my dream with its lies . <end>
<unchecked> <start> I , a creeper ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Never in my life have I crept before mighty ones . <end>
<unchecked> <start> And if I ever lied , I lied from love . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Therefore am I glad even in my wintry bed . <end>
<unchecked> <start> A poor bed warmth me better than a rich bed for I am jealous of my poverty . <end>
<unchecked> <start> And in winter it is the most faithful unto me . <end>
<unchecked> <start> With a wickedness I begin every day : I mock at the winter by a cold bath . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Therefore grumbled my stern house friend . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Besides I like to tickle him with a little wax candle so that , at last , he may let the sky come out of ashen gray dawn . <end>
<unchecked> <start> For particularly wicked am I in the morning . <end>
<unchecked> <start> At an early hour , when the pail clattered at the well , and the horses with heat whinny through gray lanes Impatiently I wait , that , at last , the clear sky may open unto me , the wintry sky with its beard of snow , the old and white headed man The wintry sky , the silent , which often even kenneth back its sun ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Have I learnt from it the long bright silence ? <end>
<unchecked> <start> Or hath it learnt it from me ? <end>
<unchecked> <start> Or hath either of us invented it himself ? <end>
<unchecked> <start> The origin of all good things is thousandfold . <end>
<unchecked> <start> From lust all good wanton things spring into existence . <end>
<unchecked> <start> How could they do so in all cases once only ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> A good wanton thing is the long silence . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Like the wintry sky , to look out of a bright face with round eyes , Like it to keep back one s sun and one s inflexible sun will verily , this art and this winter wantonness learned I well ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> My dearest wickedness and art is it , that my silence learned not to betray itself by being silent . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Rattling with words and dice I outwit those who wait solemnly . <end>
<unchecked> <start> My will and end shall escape all these severe watchers . <end>
<unchecked> <start> That no one might look down into my bottom and last will , I have invented for myself the long bright silence . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Many a prudent one I found . <end>
<unchecked> <start> He veiled his face and made muddy his water , that no one might look through it and down into it . <end>
<unchecked> <start> But just unto him the cleverer mistrustful and nutcrackers came . <end>
<unchecked> <start> They fished just out of his water his best hidden fish ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> But the bright , the brave , the transparent for me they are the wisest silent ones they , whose bottom is so deep that even the clearest water doth not betray it . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Thou silent wintry sky with thy beard of snow ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Thou white head above me with thy round eyes ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Oh , thou heavenly likeness of my soul and its wantonness ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> And must I not hide myself like one who hath swallowed gold , in order that my soul may not be cut open ? <end>
<unchecked> <start> Must I not walk on stilts in order that my long legs may escape the notice of all those envious and malicious folk around me ? <end>
<unchecked> <start> Those souls smoky , fireside warmed , used up , covered with green , sulky how could their envy endure my happiness ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> But as things are , I show them only the ice and the winter on my summits and not that my mount teeth around itself all the girdles of the sun ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> They hear the whistle of my wintry storms only and not that I also sail over warm seas , like longing , heavy , hot south winds . <end>
<unchecked> <start> They have pity on my accidents and chances . <end>
<unchecked> <start> But my word is : Let chance come unto me ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Innocent it is , as a little child ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> How could they endure my happiness if I did not put round it accidents and winter sorrows and caps of polar bear skin and covers of snowy skies ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> If I had not pity for their pity , for the pity of these envious and malicious folk ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> And if I did not . <end>
<unchecked> <start> sigh in their presence myself and chatter with cold and allow myself to be patiently wrapped in their pity ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> This is the wise wantonness and good will of my soul , that it doth not hide its winter and its snow storms neither doth it hide its chilblains . <end>
<unchecked> <start> The loneliness of the one is the flight of the sick one the loneliness of the other is the flight from the sick . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Let them hear me chatter and sigh with the winter cold , all those poor , envious rogues round me ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> With such sighing and chattering I fly from their well warmed rooms . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Let them pity me and sigh with me because of my chilblains . <end>
<unchecked> <start> At the ice of perception at last he will freeze unto death ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Thus they complain . <end>
<unchecked> <start> In the meantime with warm feet I walk crosswise and over my mount of olives . <end>
<unchecked> <start> In the sunny corner of my mount of olives I sing and mock at all pity . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Thus slowly passing through much folk and towns of many kinds by round about ways , Zarathustra returned unto his mountains and his cave . <end>
<unchecked> <start> And , behold , in doing so he came unawares unto the town gate of the great city . <end>
<unchecked> <start> But there a raging fool jumped at him with his hands spread out and stood in his way . <end>
<unchecked> <start> And this was the same fool whom the folk called the ape of Zarathustra . <end>
<unchecked> <start> For he had learnt from him some things regarding the coining and melody of speech , and borrowed probably not unwillingly from the treasure of his wisdom . <end>
<unchecked> <start> The fool thus spake unto Zarathustra : O Zarathustra , here is the great city ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Here thou hast nothing to seek and everything to lose . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Why shoulds thou wish to wade through this mud ? <end>
<unchecked> <start> Have pity on thy foot ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Rather spit at the city gate , and turn round ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Here is the hell of hermit s thoughts . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Here great thoughts are boiled alive and cooked into morsels . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Here all great feelings moulder . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Here only such little feelings are allowed to rattle as rattle from leanness . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Dost thou not smell already the shambles and cook shops of the spirit ? <end>
<unchecked> <start> Doth not this city steam with the odour of butchered spirit ? <end>
<unchecked> <start> Dost thou not see the souls hang slack like filthy rags ? <end>
<unchecked> <start> And they make even newspapers out of these rags ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Dost thou not hear how in this place the spirit hath become a play upon words ? <end>
<unchecked> <start> Loathsome is vomited by it . <end>
<unchecked> <start> And they make even newspapers out of that dishwater of words . <end>
<unchecked> <start> They hunt each other they know not whither . <end>
<unchecked> <start> They make each other hot and know not why . <end>
<unchecked> <start> They jungle with their tin foil they tinkle with their gold . <end>
<unchecked> <start> They feel cold and seek warmth for themselves in distilled waters they are hot and seek coolness in frozen spirits they are all sick and full of sores from public opinion . <end>
<unchecked> <start> All lusts and vices are here at home . <end>
<unchecked> <start> But here also are virtuous ones , here is much competent virtue in service Much competent virtue with fingers to write and hard flesh to sit and wait , blessed with small stars on the breast and stuffed small launched daughters . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Here also are much piety and much faithful and spittle baking before the God of hosts . <end>
<unchecked> <start> For from above the star dropped , and the gracious spittle . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Upwards every starless breast longer . <end>
<unchecked> <start> The moon hath her court , and the court hath its moon calves . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Unto whatever cometh from the court pray the beggar folk and all competent beggar virtue . <end>
<unchecked> <start> I serve , thou serves , we serve thus all competent virtue prayer upwards unto the prince , in order that the star which hath been deserved may at last be fixed on the narrow breast ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> But the moon revolver round all that is earthly . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Thus the prince also turned round what is earliest of all : that is the gold of shopkeepers . <end>
<unchecked> <start> The God of hosts is not a God of gold bars . <end>
<unchecked> <start> The prince thinkers , but the shopkeeper directed ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> By all that is light and strong and good within thee , O Zarathustra ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Spit on this town of shopkeepers and turn round ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Here the blood flowers rotten and lukewarm and with a scum through all veins . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Spit at the great city , which is the great rubbish heap where all the scum simmered together ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Spit at this town of the pressed in souls and the narrow breasts , the pointed eyes and the sticky fingers At this town of intruders , impudent ones , writers and bowlers , of over heated ambitious ones Where all that is tainted , feigned , lustful , lustful , over mellow , ulcer yellow , conspiring , ulcerated togetherHere Zarathustra interrupted the raging fool and shut his mouth . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Stop now ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Zarathustra cried , I have long loathed thy speech and kin ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Why hast thou dwelt so long nigh the swamp that thou wert obliged to become a frog and a toad ? <end>
<unchecked> <start> Doth not a rotten suchlike swamp blood flow through thine own veins , that thou hast learnt to croak and slander thus . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Why went thou not into the forest ? <end>
<unchecked> <start> Or why didst thou not plough the soil ? <end>
<unchecked> <start> Is not the sea full of green islands ? <end>
<unchecked> <start> I despise thy despising . <end>
<unchecked> <start> And if thou warned me , why didst thou not warn thyself ? <end>
<unchecked> <start> From love alone my despising and my warning bird shall fly up but not out of the swamp ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> They call thee mine ape , thou raging fool , but I call thee my grunting pig . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Through grunting thou even spoiled my praise of folly . <end>
<unchecked> <start> What then was it what made thee grunt first ? <end>
<unchecked> <start> Because nobody flattered thee sufficiently , therefore thou attest down at this filth in order to have reason to grunt much , In order to have reason for much revenge ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> For revenge , thou idle fool , is all thy raging . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Truly I have found thee out ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> But thy foolish word doth harm unto me even where thou art right ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> And if Zarathustra s word were even right a hundred times , with my word thou wouldst always do wrong ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Thus spake Zarathustra . <end>
<unchecked> <start> And long he gazed at the great city , sighed and was long silent . <end>
<unchecked> <start> At last he spake thus : I loathe this great city and not merely this fool . <end>
<unchecked> <start> In neither is there anything to be improved , anything to be made worse . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Alas , for this great city ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Would I could see now the pillar of fire by which it will be burnt ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> For such pillars of fire will have to precede the great noon . <end>
<unchecked> <start> But this hath its time and its own fate . <end>
<unchecked> <start> This wisdom I give thee , thou fool , at parting : Where one can love no longer , one shall pass ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Thus spake Zarathustra and passed the fool and the great city . <end>
<unchecked> <start> OF apostate Alas ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> doth everything lie withered and gray that of late stood green and many coloured on this meadow ? <end>
<unchecked> <start> And how much honey of hope carried I hence into my bee hives ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> These young hearts have all become old and not even old ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> only weary , vulgar , indolent . <end>
<unchecked> <start> They call it : We have become pious once more . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Of late I saw them run out on brave feet at early morning . <end>
<unchecked> <start> But their feet of perception have wearied , and now they even slander the bravery of their morning ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Verily , many a one of them once lifted his feet like a dancer , the laughter in my wisdom making signs unto him . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Then he changed his mind . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Just now I have seen him creep crooked unto the cross . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Round light and freedom they once fluttered , like midges and young poets . <end>
<unchecked> <start> A little older , a little colder and quickly they have become obscurantist and members and stay at homes . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Did their heart lose its courage , because loneliness devoured me like a whale ? <end>
<unchecked> <start> Did their ear hearken longingly and long in vain for me and my and herald calls ? <end>
<unchecked> <start> Alas ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> There are always but few whose heart hath a long courage and long overflowing spirits and unto such the spirit remained patient also . <end>
<unchecked> <start> But the rest are cowards ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> The rest , that meant always the great majority , the every day folk , the superabundance , the much too many all these are cowards ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Unto him who is of my kin , experiences of my kin will cross the way . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Thus his first companions must be corpses and buffoons . <end>
<unchecked> <start> But his second companions they will call themselves his faithful ones a living hive , much love , much folly , much beardless veneration . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Whoever is of my kin among men , shall not tie his heart unto these faithful ones ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Whoever knower the fleeting cowardly kind of man , shall not believe in these springs and many coloured meadows ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> If they could do otherwise , they would will otherwise also . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Half and half ones spoil every whole . <end>
<unchecked> <start> That leaves wither , why lament about that ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Let them go and fall , O Zarathustra , and lament not ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Rather blow among them with rustling winds ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Blow among these leaves , O Zarathustra , that all that is withered may still faster run away from thee ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> We have become pious once more these apostate confess and some of them are too cowardly to confess that . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Into their eye I gaze into their face and into the blushing of their cheeks I tell it : You are such as pray again ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> But it is a shame to pray ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Not for all , but for thee and me and him who hath his conscience in his head . <end>
<unchecked> <start> For thee it is a shame to pray ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Thou knows it well : thy cowardly devil within thee who would fain fold his hands and lay them in his lap and have things made easier this cowardly devil persuade thee there is a God ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Thereby thou belonged unto that kin that fear the light , that cannon find rest in the light . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Now daily thou must put thy head deeper into night and damp ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> And , verily , thou closest the hour well for just now the moths have swarmed out again . <end>
<unchecked> <start> The hour hath come for all folk that fear the light , the hour of even and rest , when they do not rest . <end>
<unchecked> <start> I hear it and smell it : their hour hath come for hunting and procession true , not for the wild huntsman , but for a hunting tame , lame , snuffling , a hunting of eavesdroppers and secret praying ones For a hunting of soul breathing sneaks . <end>
<unchecked> <start> All mousetrap for hearts have been set once more ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> And wherever I lift a curtain , a little moth rushed forth from it . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Did it squat there together with another little moth ? <end>
<unchecked> <start> For everywhere I smell little hidden communions and wherever there are small rooms , there are new bigots and the odour of bigots . <end>
<unchecked> <start> They sit for long evenings together saying : Let us become again like the little children and say dear God ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> with their mouth and stomach spoiled by the pious commit makers . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Or for long evenings they gaze at an artful , lurking cross spider that preacher prudence unto the spiders themselves and teachers thus : Below crosses there is good spinning ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Or they sit all day with fishing rods at the swamps , and thereby believe themselves profound . <end>
<unchecked> <start> But him who fisher where there are no fish I call not even superficial ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Or they learn to play the harp piously and gaily from a hymn writer , who would fain harp himself into the heart of young little women . <end>
<unchecked> <start> For he hath wearied of old little women and their praises . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Or they learn how to shudder with a learned half madman who waited in dark rooms for the spirits to come unto him and the spirit kenneth wholly away ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> And they listen unto an old juggler piper and snarled who hath wandered about and learnt from dreary winds the affliction of tones . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Now he whistle after the wind and preacher affliction in dreary tones . <end>
<unchecked> <start> And some of them have even become night watchmen . <end>
<unchecked> <start> They know now how to blow horns and to walk about in the night and awaken old things which have long ago fallen asleep . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Five words of old things I heard last night at the garden wall . <end>
<unchecked> <start> They came from such old , dreary , dry night watchmen . <end>
<unchecked> <start> For a father he taketh not care enough of his children . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Human fathers do it better ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> He is too old ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> He no longer taketh care of his children at all thus answered the other night watchman . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Hath he got children ? <end>
<unchecked> <start> No one can prove he hath , if he doth not prove it himself ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> I have wished for a long time he would prove it for once thoroughly . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Prove ? <end>
<unchecked> <start> As though he had ever proved anything ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Proof is hard for him . <end>
<unchecked> <start> He layer much stress upon folk believing him . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Ay ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Ay ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Belief maketh him blessed , belief in him . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Thus is the way of old folk ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Thus it will be with us too ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> night watchmen and shunters of the light , and afterwards drearily blew their horns . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Thus it came to pass at the garden wall . <end>
<unchecked> <start> But my heart writhed with laughter and was like to break and knew not whither to go , and sank into the midriff . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Verily , it will one day be my death that I choke with laughter , when seeing asses drunken , and hearing night watchmen thus doubt God . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Hath the time not long since passed even for all such doubts ? <end>
<unchecked> <start> Who may at this time of day awaken such old things which have fallen asleep and shunned the light ? <end>
<unchecked> <start> For the old Gods came unto an end long ago . <end>
<unchecked> <start> And , verily , it was a good and joyful end of Gods ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> They did not die lingering in the twilight , although that lie is told ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> On the contrary , they once upon a time laughed themselves unto death ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> That came to pass when by a God himself the most ungodly word was uttered , the word : There is one God ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Thou shalt have no other Gods before me ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> An old grim beard of a God , a jealous one , forgot himself thus . <end>
<unchecked> <start> And then all Gods laughed and shook on their chairs and cried : Is godliness not just that there are Gods , but no God ? <end>
<unchecked> <start> Thus spake Zarathustra in the town which he loved and which is called the Cow of Many Colours . <end>
<unchecked> <start> From it he had only two more days to walk in order to return unto his cave and his animals . <end>
<unchecked> <start> And his soul rejoiced without ceasing over the highness of his return home . <end>
<unchecked> <start> RETURN HOMEWARD Oh , loneliness ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Thou my home , loneliness ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Too long have I lived wild in wild places afar off , to be able to return home unto thee without tears ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Now threaten me with the finger , as mothers do now smile at me , as mothers do , now speak : And who was it that once upon a time like a storming rushed away from me ? <end>
<unchecked> <start> Who , taking leave , called : Too long I sat with loneliness there I unlearned silence ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Peradventure thou hast now learnt that ? <end>
<unchecked> <start> O Zarathustra ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> I know all and that thou wert more sorely forsaken among the many , thou one , than thou ever wert with me ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Forsakenness is one thing , loneliness is another that thou hast now learnt ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> And that , among men , thou wilt always be wild and strange Wild and strange even when they love thee for above all they wish to be spared ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> But here thou art in thine own home and house here thou canst speak out everything and pour out all reasons . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Nothing here is ashamed of hidden , obdurate feelings . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Here all things come fondling unto thy speech and flatter thee for they will ride on thy back . <end>
<unchecked> <start> On every likeness thou rides here unto every truth . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Upright and sincere mayfest thou here speak unto all things . <end>
<unchecked> <start> And , verily , it sounded like praise unto their ears , that one speaker frankly with all things ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Another thing , however , is . <end>
<unchecked> <start> For dost thou remember , O Zarathustra , when thy bird shrieked above thee , when thou soonest in the forest irresolute whither to go , unknowing , nigh unto a corpse ? <end>
<unchecked> <start> When thou spaces : Let mine animals lead me . <end>
<unchecked> <start> More dangerous I found it among men than among animals ? <end>
<unchecked> <start> That was ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> And dost thou remember , O Zarathustra , when thou attest on thine island among empty pails , a well of wine , giving and spending among thirsty folk , granting and pouring out Until , at last , thou attest alone thirsty among drunken folk and wailed : Is taking not more blissful than giving ? <end>
<unchecked> <start> And stealing still more blissful than taking ? <end>
<unchecked> <start> That was ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> And dost thou remember , O Zarathustra , when thy smallest hour came and drove thee away from thyself , when it spake with evil whispering : Speak and break ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> silence , and abashed thine humble courage ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> That was ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Oh , loneliness ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Thou my home , loneliness ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> How blissfully and fondly speaker thy voice unto me ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> We do not ask each other , we do not wail with each other , we openly go together through open doors . <end>
<unchecked> <start> For all is open and bright with thee , and even the hours run here on lighter feet . <end>
<unchecked> <start> For in the dark , time is a heavier burden than in the light . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Here the words of being and shrines of words of being open suddenly . <end>
<unchecked> <start> All being longer here to become language , all becoming longer here to learn to speak from me . <end>
<unchecked> <start> But down there all speech is in vain ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> There to forget and to pass by are the best wisdom . <end>
<unchecked> <start> That have I learnt now ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> He who would conceive all with men , would have to touch everything . <end>
<unchecked> <start> But for that my hands are too clean . <end>
<unchecked> <start> I do not like to breathe even their breath . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Alas , that I have lived so long amid their noise and bad breath ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Oh , blissful stillness round me ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Oh , pure odours round me ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Oh , how this stillness bridget pure breath out of a deep breast ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Oh , how it hearken eth , this blissful stillness ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> But down there everything speaker , everything is overheard . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Let folk proclaim their wisdom bringing bells , the shopkeepers in the market will outing them with their pennies ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Everything with them speaker , no one knower how to understand . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Everything fallen into the water , nothing fallen into deep wells any more . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Everything with them speaker , nothing more succeeded and cometh unto an end . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Everything doth cackle , but who will sit still on the nest and hatch eggs ? <end>
<unchecked> <start> Everything with them speaker , everything is spoken into pieces . <end>
<unchecked> <start> And what yesterday was too hard for time itself and its tooth , to day hanged out of the mouths of the folk of to day scraped and gnawed into pieces . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Everything with them speaker , everything is betrayed . <end>
<unchecked> <start> And what once was called secret and a secrecy of deep souls , to day belonged unto the trumpeters of the streets and other butterflies . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Oh , human kind , how strange thou art ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Thou noise in dark lanes ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Now thou again list behind me ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> My greatest danger lieth behind me ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> In sparing and pity lay always my greatest danger and all human kind wished to be spared and endured . <end>
<unchecked> <start> With truths kept back , with a foolish hand and a fooled heart , and rich with the small lies of pity thus have I always lived among men . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Disguised I sat among them , ready to mistake myself in order to endure them , and willingly trying to persuade myself : Thou fool , thou dost not know men ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> One unlearned men when living among men . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Too much foreground is in all men what could far seeing , far searching eyes do there ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> And when they mistook me fool that I was , I spared them on that account more than I spared myself ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> For I was accustomed to be hard upon myself , and often even took revenge on myself for that sparing . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Stung all over by poisonous flies , and hollowed like a stone by many drops of wickedness , I sat among them and tried to persuade myself : Innocent of its smallness is everything small ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Especially those who call themselves the good I found to be the most poisonous flies . <end>
<unchecked> <start> They sting in all innocence , they lie in all innocence . <end>
<unchecked> <start> How could they be just unto me ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Whoever giveth among the good , is taught to lie by pity . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Pity maketh the air damp unto all free souls . <end>
<unchecked> <start> For the stupidity of the good is unfathomable . <end>
<unchecked> <start> To hide myself and my riches that I have learnt down there for every one I found to be poor in spirit . <end>
<unchecked> <start> That was the lie of my pity , that I knew about everyone , That I saw and smelt at once in everyone how much of spirit was enough for him , and how much of spirit was too much for him ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Their stiff wise men I called them wise , not stiff . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Thus I learned to swallow words . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Their grave diggers I called them searchers and examiners . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Thus I learned to exchange words . <end>
<unchecked> <start> The grave diggers get sicknesses by digging . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Under old rubbish there rest bad odours . <end>
<unchecked> <start> One must not stir up the swamp . <end>
<unchecked> <start> One must live on mountains . <end>
<unchecked> <start> With blessed nostrils I breathe again . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Saved , at last , is my nose from the odour of all human kind ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Tickled by sharp breezes , as it were by sparkling wines , my soul sneeze . <end>
<unchecked> <start> It sneeze and in triumph cried : God bless me ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> In a dream , in a last dream of the morning , I stood this day on a promontory , beyond the world . <end>
<unchecked> <start> I held a balance and weighed the world . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Alas , that the dawn came too soon unto me ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> It walked me by its glow , the jealous one ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Jealous is it always of the glow of my morning dreams . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Measurable for him who hath time for a good weigher reachable by the flight of strong wings by godlike nut crackers thus my dream found the world to be . <end>
<unchecked> <start> My dream , a bold sailor , half ship , half whirlwind , silent as butterflies , impatient as a falcon gentle why had it this day patience and leisure to weigh the world ? <end>
<unchecked> <start> Did my wisdom silently speak unto it , my laughing , wide awake wisdom of daylight which pocket at all infinite worlds ? <end>
<unchecked> <start> For it saith : Where there is force , there the number become master for it hath the greater force . <end>
<unchecked> <start> How securely did my dream look on this finite world , not curious , not greedy for old things , not afraid , not praying . <end>
<unchecked> <start> As if a round apple was offered unto my hand , a ripe , golden apple , with a cool , smooth , velvet skin thus the world offered itself unto me . <end>
<unchecked> <start> As if a tree made a sign unto me , with broad boughs and a strong will , bent for the weary wanderer to lean against and use as a foot stool thus stood the world on my promontory . <end>
<unchecked> <start> As if neat hands carried towards me a chest , a chest open for the rapture of bashful reversing eyes thus the world this day offered itself unto me . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Not riddle enough to frighten away human love not solution enough to put to sleep human wisdom a humanly good thing for me , this day , was the world of which such bad things are said ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> How thankful am I unto my morning dream because I thus weighed the world early this morning ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> As a humanly good thing it came unto me , this dream and comforter of the heart ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> And in order to do during the day what it did , and to learn from it its best , now I will put the three vilest things on the balance and weigh them in a humanly good spirit ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> He who taught to bless , taught also to curse . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Which are the three best cursed things in the world ? <end>
<unchecked> <start> These I will put on the balance . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Voluptuousness , thirst of power , selfishness these three have hitherto been cursed best and have had the worst renown and been worst . <end>
<unchecked> <start> These three I will weigh in a humanly good spirit . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Up ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Here is my promontory , and there is the sea . <end>
<unchecked> <start> That rolled nigh unto me , with shaggy hair , flatteringly , the faithful old dog monster with an hundred heads which I love . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Up ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Here will I keep the balance over the rolling sea ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> And a witness I choose also , to look at my weighing , thee , thou hermit tree , which I love , with thy strong odour and thy broad arching boughs . <end>
<unchecked> <start> On what bridge doth the Now go unto the one-day ? <end>
<unchecked> <start> By what compulsion doth what is high compel itself to join what is low ? <end>
<unchecked> <start> And what bidder even the highest grow upwards ? <end>
<unchecked> <start> Now the balance standish equal and still . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Three heavy questions I have thrown into it three heavy answers are carried by the other scale . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Voluptuousness unto all despises of the body who wear penance shirts , a sting and stake , and cursed as a world by all back worlds men . <end>
<unchecked> <start> For it pocket at , and maketh fools of , all teachers of confusion and heresy . <end>
<unchecked> <start> which they are burnt for all worm eaten wood , for all stinking rags , the ready oven of love fire and stewing . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Voluptuousness for free hearts innocent and free , the garden joy of earth , the overflowing thankfulness of all the future towards the present . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Voluptuousness a sweet poison unto the withered only , but the great invigoration of the heart and the reverently spared wine of wines for those who have the will of a lion . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Voluptuousness the great prototype of a higher happiness and the highest hope . <end>
<unchecked> <start> For unto many things matrimony is promised and more than matrimony , Unto many things which are stranger unto each other than man and woman are . <end>
<unchecked> <start> And who would perceive completely how strange man and woman are unto each other ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Voluptuousness but I will have railings round my thoughts and even round my words , that swine and enthusiasts may not break into my gardens ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Thirst of power the glowing scourge of the hardest in hardness of heart the horrid torture reserved for the very cruellest the gloomy flame of living pyre . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Thirst of power the malicious gadfly which is being set on the finest peoples the corner of all uncertain virtue that which ride on every horse and on every pride . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Thirst of power the earthquake that breath , and by breaking opened , all that is rotten and hollow the rolling , grudging , punishing breaker of whited sepulchres the shining interrogation mark beside premature answers . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Thirst of power before the glance of which man creepers and bucket and slave and become lower than serpent or swine , until at last the great contempt cried out of him . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Thirst of power the terrible teacher of the great contempt which preacher : Away with thee ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> in the very face of cities and empires , until a cry cometh out of themselves : Away with me ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Thirst of power which alluring mounted heights up unto the pure and lonely , glowing like a love that , alluring , painted purple blesses on earthly heavens . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Thirst of power but who could call it thirst , if what is high longer to step down for power ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Verily , there is nothing sick or supportive in such a longing and stepping down ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> That the lonely height may not for ever be lonely and self contented that the mount may come unto the valley , and the winds of the height unto the low lands ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Oh ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> who could find the right Christian name and name of virtue for such a longing ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Giving virtue thus the unutterable was once called by Zarathustra . <end>
<unchecked> <start> And then it also came to pass and , verily , to pass for the first time , that his word praised blessed selfishness , whole , healthy selfishness that springett from a mighty soul From a mighty soul , part of which is the high body , the beautiful , victorious , recreative , round which every thing become a mirror The flexible , persuading body , the dancer whose likeness and summary is the self joyful soul . <end>
<unchecked> <start> The of such bodies and souls called itself virtue . <end>
<unchecked> <start> With its words of good and bad such a self joy protected itself as with sacred groves . <end>
<unchecked> <start> With the names of its happiness it banished from itself all that is contemptible . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Away from itself it banished all that is cowardly . <end>
<unchecked> <start> It saith : Bad that meant cowardly ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Contemptible appeared unto it the ever sorrowful , sighing , miserable one , and whoever collected even the smallest advantage . <end>
<unchecked> <start> It despised all wisdom happy in misery . <end>
<unchecked> <start> For , verily , there is also wisdom that flourished in darkness , a wisdom of nightlife shadows which ever sight : All is vain ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> The shy distrusting is regarded as inferior , and whoever wanted oaths instead of looks and hands including all all too mistrustful wisdom for such is the way of cowardly souls . <end>
<unchecked> <start> As lower still it regarded him who is quick to oblige , dog like , who at once lieth down on his back , who is submissive and there is also wisdom that is submissive and dog like and pious and quick to oblige . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Hateful and loathsome unto it is he who gareth not to defend himself , who swallowed down poisonous spittle and evil looks , the all too patient one , the sufferer of everything , the all too contented one for that is the way of slaves . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Whether one be servile before Gods and divine kicks whether he be so before men and silly human opinions at all the slave tribe it , that blessed selfishness ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Bad thus it called all that is broken and , unfree blinking eyes , pressed down hearts , and that false yielding tribe that kissed with broad cowardly lips . <end>
<unchecked> <start> And spurious wisdom thus selfishness called all the quibbles of slaves and old men and weary ones and in particular the whole bad , mad , over witty ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> The spurious wise men , however , all the priests , the weary of the world , and those whose souls are of the tribe of women and slaves , oh ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> how well hath their play ever abused selfishness ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> And this very thing , to ill use selfishness , was proclaimed to be virtue and to be called virtue ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> And unselfish thus , with good reason , all those cowards weary of the world and cross spiders wished to be ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> And for all those the day now cometh , the change , the sword of judgment , the great noon . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Then much shall become apparent ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> And he who proclaimed the I whole and holy , and selfishness blessed , a prophet indeed , saith also what he knower : Behold , it cometh , it is nigh , the great noon f My gift of the gab is of the folk . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Too coarsely and heartily for angora rabbits I speak . <end>
<unchecked> <start> And still stranger my word sounded unto all ink fish and lennoxes . <end>
<unchecked> <start> My hand is a fool s hand . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Woe unto all tables and walls , and whatever hath space left for fools ornaments , fools scribbling ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> My foot is a horse s foot . <end>
<unchecked> <start> With it I trample and trot over logs and stones , crosswise and straight over the fields , and I am the devil s with lust in all my fast running . <end>
<unchecked> <start> My stomach is it an eagle s stomach ? <end>
<unchecked> <start> For it lieth best to eat lamb s flesh . <end>
<unchecked> <start> But certainly it is a bird s stomach . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Fed on innocent and few things , ready and impatient to fly , to fly away that is now my way . <end>
<unchecked> <start> How should not something of birds ways be in it ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> And , in particular , that I am an enemy unto the spirit of gravity , that is a bird s way . <end>
<unchecked> <start> And , verily , a mortal enemy , an arch enemy , a born fiend ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Oh ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> whither hath mine enmity not already flown and strayed ? <end>
<unchecked> <start> Of that I could sing a song and will sing it , although I am alone in an empty house and must sing it unto mine own ears . <end>
<unchecked> <start> True , there are other singers , whose throat is softened only , whose hand become talkative only , whose eye expressive only , whose heart awake only , when the hall is well filled . <end>
<unchecked> <start> I am not like unto them . <end>
<unchecked> <start> He who one day will teach men to fly , hath moved all landmarks . <end>
<unchecked> <start> All landmarks will themselves fly into the air , the earth will be baptised anew by that man as the light one . <end>
<unchecked> <start> The ostrich bird kenneth faster than the swiftest horse , but even it putter its head heavily into the heavy ground . <end>
<unchecked> <start> So doth man who cannon yet fly . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Earth and life are called heavy by him thus the spirit of gravity willett ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> But whoever intended to become light and a bird , must love himself thus teach . <end>
<unchecked> <start> True , not with the love of the sick and supportive . <end>
<unchecked> <start> For with them stinker even love unto themselves ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> One must learn how to love one s self thus I teach with a whole and healthy love , that one may find life with one s self endurable , and not go gadding about . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Such a gadding about baptist itself love unto one s neighbour . <end>
<unchecked> <start> With this word folk have lied best hitherto and dissemble best , and in particular those whom all the world felt to be heavy . <end>
<unchecked> <start> And , verily , it is no commandment for to day and to morrow , to learn how to love one s self . <end>
<unchecked> <start> It is rather the finest , , last and most patient of arts . <end>
<unchecked> <start> For unto him who possessed it , all that is possessed is well hidden and of all treasure pits one s own is dogged out last . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Thus the spirit of gravity cause it to be . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Almost in the cradle we are given heavy words and values . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Good and evil that cradle gift is called . <end>
<unchecked> <start> For its sake we are forgiven for living . <end>
<unchecked> <start> And for that end one called the little children unto one s self , to forbid them in good time to love themselves . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Thus the spirit of gravity cause it to be . <end>
<unchecked> <start> And we we carry faithfully what we are given , on hard shoulders over rough mountains ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> And when perspiring , we are told : Yea , life is hard to bear ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> But man himself only is hard to bear ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> The reason is that he carried too many strange things on his shoulders . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Like the camel he kneeled down and allowed the heavy load to be put on his backing particular the strong man who is able to bear the load , who is possessed by reverence . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Too many strange , heavy words and values he taketh upon his shoulders . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Now life appeared unto him to be a desert . <end>
<unchecked> <start> And , verily ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Even many things that are one s own , are hard to bear ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> And many inward things in man are like unto the oyster , i . <end>
<unchecked> <start> e . <end>
<unchecked> <start> loathsome and slippery and difficult to catch So that a noble shell with noble ornaments must plead for it . <end>

<unchecked> <start> THUS SPAKE ZARATHUSTRA , III They are contrary unto my taste all the publicans and shopkeepers and kings and other keepers of lands and shops . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Verily , I have also learnt to wait , and from the bottom , but only to wait for myself . <end>
<unchecked> <start> And I learned to stand and to walk and to run and to jump and to climb and to dance over all things . <end>
<unchecked> <start> But this is my teaching : whoever wished to learn to fly one day , must first learn to stand and walk and run and climb and dance . <end>
<unchecked> <start> One doth not learn flying by flying ! <end>

<unchecked> <start> Alas , that his best is so very small ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> The great loathing of man , it choked me , it had crept into my throat and what the fortune teller foretold : All is equal , nothing is worth while , knowledge choked . <end>
<unchecked> <start> A long dawn limped in front of me , a sadness weary unto death , drunken from death , and speaking with a yawning mouth . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Eternally he recurrent , man , of whom thou wear est , the small man . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Thus yawned my sadness and dragged its foot and could not fall asleep . <end>
<unchecked> <start> A cave became the human earth for me , its chest fell in , all that giveth became unto me mould of men and bones and a rotten past . <end>
<unchecked> <start> My sighing sat on all human graves and could no longer get up my sighing and questioning cried like a toad , and choked , and gnawed , and complained by day and night : Alas , man recurrent eternally ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> The small man recurrent eternally ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Once I had seen both naked , the greatest man and the smallest man all too like unto each other all nonhuman even the greatest man ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> All too small the greatest one ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> That was my satiety of man ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> And eternal recurrence even of the smallest one ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> That was my satiety of all existence . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Alas ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> loathing ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> loathing ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> loathing ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Thus spake Zarathustra , and sighed and shuddered for he remembered his illness . <end>
<unchecked> <start> But his animals would not allow him to speak further . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Speak not further , thou convalescent one ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Thus his animals answered . <end>
<unchecked> <start> But go out where the world waited for thee like a garden . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Go out unto the roses and bees and flocks of doves ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> But especially unto the singing birds , that thou mayfest learn singing from them ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> For singing is good for the convalescent the healthy one may speak . <end>
<unchecked> <start> And when the healthy one wanted songs also , he wanted other songs than the convalescent one . <end>
<unchecked> <start> O ye buffoons and barrel organs , be silent ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Zarathustra answered and smiled at his animals . <end>
<unchecked> <start> How will ye know what comfort I invented for myself in seven days ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> That I was compelled to sing again that comfort I invented for myself and that convalescence . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Are ye going to make at once a barrel organ song even out of that ? <end>
<unchecked> <start> Speak no further , his animals answered once more . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Rather , thou convalescent one , make first a lyre , a new lyre ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> For , behold , O Zarathustra ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> For thy new songs new tyres are requisite . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Sing and foam over , O Zarathustra , heal thy soul with new songs , that thou mayfest carry thy great fate that hath not yet been any man s fate ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> For thine animals know well , O Zarathustra , who thou art and must become . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Behold , thou art the teacher of eternal recurrence . <end>
<unchecked> <start> That is now thy fate ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> That thou hast to be the first to teach this doctrine how should this great fate not also be thy greatest danger and illness ? <end>
<unchecked> <start> Behold , we know what thou teaches that all things recur eternally , ourselves included and that we have been there infinite times before , and all things with us . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Thou teaches that there is a great year of becoming , a monstrous , great year . <end>
<unchecked> <start> It must , like an hour glass , ever turn upside down again in order to run down and run out So that all these years are like unto each other , in the greatest and in the smallest things so that in every great year we are like unto ourselves , in the greatest and in the smallest things . <end>
<unchecked> <start> And if thou wouldst now die , O Zarathustra behold , we even know what thou wouldst then say unto thyself . <end>
<unchecked> <start> But thine animals pray thee not to die yet ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Thou wouldst speak , and without trembling , on the contrary breathing deeply with happiness . <end>
<unchecked> <start> For a great burden and saltiness would be taken from thee , thou most patient one ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Now I die and vanish , thou wouldst say , and in a moment I shall be nothing . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Souls are as mortal as bodies . <end>
<unchecked> <start> But the knot of causes recurrent in which I am twined . <end>
<unchecked> <start> It will create me again ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> I myself belong unto the causes of eternal recurrence . <end>
<unchecked> <start> I come back , with this sun , with this earth , with this eagle , with this serpent not for a new life , or a better life , or an eternal life . <end>
<unchecked> <start> I come eternally back unto this one and the same life , in the greatest things and in the smallest things , in order to teach once more the eternal recurrence of all things In order to speak again the word of the great noon of earth and man in order to proclaim again beyond man unto man . <end>
<unchecked> <start> I have spoken my word I break from my word . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Thus willett mine eternal fate . <end>
<unchecked> <start> As a proclaimed I perish ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> The hour hath come now , when the perishing one blessed himself . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Thus ended Zarathustra s destruction . <end>
<unchecked> <start> The animals having said these words , were silent and waited to see whether Zarathustra would say anything unto them . <end>
<unchecked> <start> But Zarathustra did not hear that they were silent . <end>
<unchecked> <start> On the contrary : he lay still , with his eyes closed , like one asleep , although he did not sleep . <end>
<unchecked> <start> For he was communing with his soul . <end>
<unchecked> <start> But the serpent and the eagle , finding him thus silent , respected the great stillness round him and cautiously withdrew . <end>
<unchecked> <start> OF GREAT LONGING O my soul , I taught thee to say to day , as well as once and long ago , and to dance thy jig over all Here and There and Elsewhere . <end>
<unchecked> <start> O my soul , I redeemed thee from all corners ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> I brushed down from thee dust , spiders and twilight . <end>
<unchecked> <start> O my soul , I washed the small shame and corner virtue down from thee , and persuaded thee to stand naked before the eyes of the sun ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> With the storm which is called spirit , I blew over thine undulating sea . <end>
<unchecked> <start> All clouds I blew away and throttled even the throttle called sin . <end>
<unchecked> <start> O my soul , I gave thee the right to say Nay like the storm , and to say Yea as the open sky doth ! <end>

<unchecked> <start> O my soul , I gave thee back freedom over created and not created things ! <end>

<unchecked> <start> O my soul , every sun I poured out over thee , and every night , and every silence , and every longing ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Then thou greatest up unto me like a vine plant . <end>
<unchecked> <start> O my soul , over rich and heavy thou stands there , a vine plant with swelling udders and close brown grapes Close and pressed from thy happiness , waiting because of abundance , and bashful even because of thy waiting . <end>
<unchecked> <start> O my soul , there is certainly now here a soul more full of love , readier to embrace and more comprehensive ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Where could the future and what is past be closer together than with thee ? <end>

<unchecked> <start> now thou safest unto me , smiling and full of melancholy : Which of us hath to thank the other ? <end>

<unchecked> <start> But his hair grew white . <end>
<unchecked> <start> One day , when he sat on a stone before his cave and silently gazed there one looked out on the sea and away over winding bosses his animals went thoughtfully round him and at last stood in front of him . <end>
<unchecked> <start> O Zarathustra , they said , dost thou look out for thy happiness ? <end>
<unchecked> <start> What is happiness worth ? <end>
<unchecked> <start> he answered . <end>
<unchecked> <start> For a long time I have not ceased to strive for my happiness now I strive for my work . <end>
<unchecked> <start> O Zarathustra , the animals said once more , Thou safest so as one who hath more than enough of what is good . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Dost thou not lie in a sky blue lake of happiness ? <end>
<unchecked> <start> Ye buffoons , answered Zarathustra smiling , how well ye chose that simile ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> But ye also know that my happiness is heavy , and is not like a liquid wave of water . <end>
<unchecked> <start> It pressed me , and will not part from me , and behave like melted pitch . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Then the animals again went thoughtfully round him and once more stood in front of him . <end>
<unchecked> <start> O Zarathustra , they said , we see , it is for that reason that thou protest ever yellower and darker , though thy hair will soon look white and flax ? <end>

<unchecked> <start> What say ye now , mine animals ? <end>
<unchecked> <start> said Zarathustra laughing . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Verily , I reviled when speaking of pitch . <end>

<unchecked> <start> The honey in my veins thickened my blood and stilled my soul also . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Thus it will be , O Zarathustra ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> answered the animals and thronged round him . <end>
<unchecked> <start> But art thou not going up a high mountain to day ? <end>
<unchecked> <start> The air is pure , and this day one teeth more of the world than ever before . <end>

<unchecked> <start> For learn , at the top I am going to make the honey offering . <end>
<unchecked> <start> But when Zarathustra had reached the summit , he sent home his animals which had led him , and found that now he was alone . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Then he laughed from the bottom of his heart , looked round and spake thus . <end>
<unchecked> <start> That I spake of offering and of honey offerings , was merely my stratagem of speech , and , verily , a useful stupidity ! <end>

<unchecked> <start> I waste what I am given . <end>
<unchecked> <start> A waster with a thousand hands am I . <end>

<unchecked> <start> And when I asked for honey , I merely wanted to have a bait and sweet slime and phlegm , for which even growling bears and strange , morose , evil birds smack their lips To have the best bait that is requisite for huntsmen and fishers . <end>
<unchecked> <start> For if the world is like a dark forest of animals , and a pleasure ground of all wild huntsmen , it seemeth unto me to be still more , and preferably , a bottomless , rich sea A sea full of many coloured fish and crabs , by which even Gods might be tempted to become fishers there and throw out their nets . <end>
<unchecked> <start> So rich is the world in strange things , great and small ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> In particular the world of men , the sea of men ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> For that I now throw out my golden fishing rod , saying : Open , O thou abyss of men ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Open and throw into my hands thy fish and glittering crabs ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> With my best bait this day I bait the strangest human fish ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> My happiness itself I throw out into all distances and remote places , between east , south , and west , to try whether on the hook of my happiness many human fish will learn to pull and wriggle . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Until they , biting on my pointed hidden hooks , are forced to come up unto my height , the most many coloured abyss grounding , unto the most malicious one of all catchers of human fish . <end>
<unchecked> <start> For this I am from the bottom and from the beginning , pulling , pulling unto me , pulling up unto me , bringing up a puller , breeder and governor , who not in vain once counselled himself : Become what thou art ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Thus men may now come up unto me . <end>
<unchecked> <start> For I am still waiting for the signs indicating that it is time for my going down . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Not yet do I perish among men , as I must do . <end>
<unchecked> <start> For that I wait here , artful and mocking on high mountains , not impatient , not patient on the contrary , one who hath among other things unlearn patience , because he suffered no more . <end>
<unchecked> <start> For my fate allowed me plenty of time . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Did it forget me ? <end>
<unchecked> <start> Or doth it sit behind a large stone in the shadow catching flies ? <end>
<unchecked> <start> And , verily , I am well disposed towards it , towards mine eternal fate , for that reason that it doth not hunt and press me , but leave me time for fibs and tricks so that this day I have gone up this high mountain to catch fish . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Hath ever a man caught fish on high mountains ? <end>
<unchecked> <start> And though what I seek and do up here be a folly , it is better to do this than by waiting down there to become solemn and green and yellow To become by waiting a sprawling one who paneth for wrath , a holy howling storm from the mountains , an impatient one who shouted down into the valleys : Listen , otherwise I shall whip you with the scourge of God ! <end>

<unchecked> <start> THE CRY FOR HELP The following day Zarathustra sat again on his stone before the cave , while the animals strayed outside in the world in order to bring home fresh food , including fresh honey . <end>
<unchecked> <start> For the old honey had been spent and wasted unto the last drop by Zarathustra . <end>
<unchecked> <start> But when he thus sat there with a stick in his hand , and copied the shadow of his figure on the ground , meditating and , verily , not upon himself and his shadow , suddenly he was terrified and gave a start . <end>
<unchecked> <start> For beside his shadow he saw another shadow . <end>
<unchecked> <start> And when he looked round quickly and arose , behold , there the fortune teller stood beside him , the same unto whom he once had given food and drink at his table , the announcer of the great weariness , who taught : Everything is equal nothing is worth while the world is without sense knowledge choked . <end>
<unchecked> <start> But in the meantime his face had changed . <end>
<unchecked> <start> And when Zarathustra looked into his eyes , his heart was terrified once more . <end>

<unchecked> <start> Zarathustra had not yet been an hour on his way through his mountains and forests , when all at once he saw a strange procession . <end>

<unchecked> <start> But now it holder him tight whom thou seeks , myself , Zarathustra . <end>
<unchecked> <start> It is I , ungodly Zarathustra , who say : Who is than I , that I may enjoy his teaching ? <end>
<unchecked> <start> Thus spake Zarathustra and pierced with his glance the thoughts and back thoughts of the old pope , who at last began : He who loved him and possessed him most , hath now lost him most ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Behold , I myself am probably at present of us two the one ? <end>
<unchecked> <start> But who could rejoice over that ? <end>
<unchecked> <start> Thou served him unto the very last asked Zarathustra thoughtfully after a deep silence , thou knows , how he died ? <end>
<unchecked> <start> Is it true what folk say , that he was suffocated by pity ? <end>
<unchecked> <start> That he saw how man hung on the cross , and could not endure that his love unto man should become his hell and at last his death ? <end>
<unchecked> <start> But the old pope answered not , but gazed aside shyly and with sullen cheer . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Let him go , said Zarathustra after long meditation , still gazing straight into the old man s eye . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Let him go , he is gone . <end>
<unchecked> <start> And although it doth honour unto thee that thou speaker well of this dead one , thou knows , as I do , who he was , and that he went strange ways . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Spoken under three eyes , said the old pope cheerfully for he was blind of an eye , in matters of God I am more enlightened than Zarathustra himself , and may well be so . <end>
<unchecked> <start> My love served him long years my will followed all his will . <end>
<unchecked> <start> And a good servant knower everything , and even many things which his master hide from himself . <end>
<unchecked> <start> He was a hidden God , full of secrecy . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Verily , even his son he begat not otherwise than by a secret way . <end>
<unchecked> <start> At the door of belief in him standish adultery . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Whoever praise him as a God of love , thinkers not highly enough of love itself . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Did that God not also wish to be a judge ? <end>
<unchecked> <start> But the loving one lovett beyond reward and retaliation . <end>
<unchecked> <start> When he was young , that God from the East , he was hard and vengeful , and built up his hell for the delight of those he loved best . <end>
<unchecked> <start> But at last he grew old and soft and mellow and full of pity , more like a grandfather than a father , but most like a shaky old grandmother . <end>
<unchecked> <start> There he sat , withered , at his fireside , grieved because of his weak legs , weary of the world , weary of will , and one day suffocated by his all too great pity . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Thou old pope , said Zarathustra interrupting , hast thou seen that with thine own eyes ? <end>
<unchecked> <start> It might have come to pass like that like that , and otherwise as well . <end>
<unchecked> <start> When Gods die , they always die divers kinds of death . <end>

<unchecked> <start> This way or that , this way and that he is gone ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> He was contrary unto the taste of mine ears and eyes . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Worse I should not like to say of him . <end>
<unchecked> <start> I love everything that gareth brightly and speaker honestly . <end>
<unchecked> <start> But he thou knows well , thou old priest , there was something of thy tribe in him , of the priestly tribe . <end>
<unchecked> <start> He had many meanings . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Besides , he was indistinct . <end>
<unchecked> <start> How angry he was with us , this out breather of wrath , because he thought we understood him ill . <end>
<unchecked> <start> But why did he not speak more cleanly ? <end>
<unchecked> <start> And if the fault was of our ears , why did he give us ears that heard badly ? <end>
<unchecked> <start> And if there was mud in our ears , go to ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> who had put it there ? <end>
<unchecked> <start> In too many things he failed , this potter who had not served his apprenticeship ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> But in taking revenge on his pots and creations , for having turned out ill , he committed a sin against good taste . <end>
<unchecked> <start> There is good taste in piety . <end>
<unchecked> <start> also . <end>
<unchecked> <start> And at last that good taste said : Away with such a God ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Rather have no God , rather be a fate for one s self , rather be a fool , rather be God one s self ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> What do I hear ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> said then the old pope , pricking up his ears O Zarathustra , thou art more pious than thou believes , with such an unbelief ! <end>

<unchecked> <start> Is it not thy piety itself that letter thee no longer believe in a God ? <end>
<unchecked> <start> And thine over great honesty will one day lead thee even beyond good and evil ! <end>

<unchecked> <start> They have been predestined from eternity for bestowing benediction . <end>
<unchecked> <start> One bestowed benediction not with the hand alone . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Although thou wouldst have thyself the one , I perceive , when thou art nigh , a secret , holy , and goodly smell of long benediction . <end>
<unchecked> <start> From it I feel weal and woe . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Let me be thy guest , O Zarathustra , for a single night ! <end>

<unchecked> <start> Amen ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> So let it be ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> said Zarathustra in great astonishment . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Up there leader the way there lieth the cave of Zarathustra . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Verily , with joy would I lead thee there myself , thou venerable one for I love all pious men . <end>
<unchecked> <start> But now a cry for help called me in haste away from thee . <end>
<unchecked> <start> In my province no one shall suffer injury . <end>
<unchecked> <start> My cave is a good harbour . <end>
<unchecked> <start> And best of all would I like to set every sad one on firm land and on firm legs once more . <end>
<unchecked> <start> But who would take thy melancholy off thy shoulders ? <end>
<unchecked> <start> For that I am too weak . <end>
<unchecked> <start> A long time , verily , we should have to wait before one would re awaken thy God . <end>
<unchecked> <start> For this old God giveth no more . <end>
<unchecked> <start> He is quite dead . <end>
<unchecked> <start> And again Zarathustra s feet traversed the hills and mountains , and his eyes sought and sought , but nowhere could they find him whom they longed to see , the great sufferer and crier for help . <end>
<unchecked> <start> But all the way he rejoiced in his heart and was grateful . <end>
