<unchecked> <start> What strange speech makers I found ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Over their words I will now chew for a long time , as over good corn . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Into morsels shall my tooth grind them and crush them , until they flow into my soul like milk ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> But when the road again went round a rock , at once the landscape changed , and Zarathustra entered a kingdom of death . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Here black and red cliffs faced sternly upwards . <end>
<unchecked> <start> No grass , no tree , no voice of bird . <end>
<unchecked> <start> For it was a valley , shunned by all animals , even by the beasts of prey . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Only a kind of ugly , thick , green snakes came thither , when they grew old , in order to die . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Therefore that valley was called by the herdsmen Death of Snakes . <end>
<unchecked> <start> But Zarathustra sank into dark recollections , for he felt as though he had stood in this valley once before . <end>
<unchecked> <start> And many heavy things lay upon his mind , so that he walked slowly and ever more slowly , and finally stood still . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Then , suddenly , opening his eyes , he saw sitting on the wayside a something shaped like a man , but scarcely like a man , a something unutterable . <end>
<unchecked> <start> And straightway Zarathustra was seized by a great shame for having cast his eyes upon such a thing . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Blushing up unto his white hair , he turned his look aside , and lifted his foot to leave that evil spot . <end>
<unchecked> <start> But then the dead desert took voice . <end>
<unchecked> <start> For from the ground something gushed up gurgling and rattling , as water in the night gurgle and rattled through stopped water pipes . <end>
<unchecked> <start> And at last that something developed into a human voice and a human speech which sounded thus : Zarathustra ! <end>

<unchecked> <start> Read my riddle ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Speak , speak ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> What is the revenge on the witness ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> I tempt thee to return . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Here is smooth ice ! <end>

<unchecked> <start> Thou seemed wise unto thyself , O proud Zarathustra ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Read the riddle , read it , thou hard cracker of nuts , the riddle which I am ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Say , say : who am If But when Zarathustra had heard these words , what think ye happened then unto his soul ? <end>
<unchecked> <start> Pity attacked him . <end>
<unchecked> <start> And all at once he fell down like an oak tree that hath long resisted many wood cutters , heavily , suddenly , unto the terror even of those about to fell it . <end>

<unchecked> <start> Let me go ! <end>

<unchecked> <start> he said at last . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Stay ! <end>

<unchecked> <start> I would be proud and rejoice at it ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> persecuted ? <end>
<unchecked> <start> And whoever persecuted well , learned easily how to follow . <end>

<unchecked> <start> That way is bad . <end>

<unchecked> <start> I trample unto death , and ruin all roads . <end>

<unchecked> <start> Thy blushing , O Zarathustra , honoured me ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> With much trouble I have got away from the thronging of the pitiful , in order to find the only one who teachers to day : Pity is an intruder . <end>
<unchecked> <start> To find thyself , O Zarathustra ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Be it a God s , be it men s pity : pity is contrary unto shame . <end>
<unchecked> <start> And not to will to help may be nobler than that virtue which readily giveth assistance . <end>
<unchecked> <start> But that is to day called virtue indeed by all petty folk : namely , pity . <end>
<unchecked> <start> They feel no reverence for great misfortune , for great ugliness , for great failure . <end>

<unchecked> <start> They are petty gray folk , with good wool and good will . <end>

<unchecked> <start> Too long have they been admitted to be right , these petty folk . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Thus at last they have also been given power . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Now they teach : Good is only what the petty folk approve . <end>
<unchecked> <start> And it is to day called truth what that preacher hath said , who sprung from themselves , that strange saint and advocate of the petty folk who proclaimed of himself : I I am the truth . <end>
<unchecked> <start> This immodest one hath now for a long time reared the crest of the petty folk he who taught no small error when he taught : I am the truth . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Hath an immodest one ever been answered more politely ? <end>

<unchecked> <start> Nay ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Three times Nay ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Thou didst warn folk of his error , thou wert the first to warn against pity not all , not none , but thyself and thy tribe . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Thou art ashamed of the shame of the great sufferer . <end>

<unchecked> <start> For many are on the way unto thee , many suffering , doubting , despairing , drowning , cold folk . <end>

<unchecked> <start> Thou hast found out my best , my worst riddle , myself and what I had done . <end>
<unchecked> <start> I know the axe that layer thee low . <end>
<unchecked> <start> But He was compelled to die . <end>
<unchecked> <start> He looked at things with eyes that saw everything . <end>
<unchecked> <start> He saw the depths and bosses of man , all his hidden shame and ugliness . <end>
<unchecked> <start> His pity knew no shame . <end>
<unchecked> <start> He crept into my foulest corners . <end>
<unchecked> <start> This most curious , over officious , over pitiful one was compelled to die . <end>
<unchecked> <start> He always saw myself . <end>
<unchecked> <start> On such a witness I wished to take revenge , or rather not to live at all ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> The God who saw everything , including man this God was compelled to die ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Man endure not that such a witness should live . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Thus spake the ugliest man . <end>
<unchecked> <start> But Zarathustra got up and prepared to depart . <end>
<unchecked> <start> For he was shuddering unto his very bowels . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Thou unutterable one , said he , thou didst warn me against thy road . <end>
<unchecked> <start> In thanks for that I praise mine unto thee . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Behold , up that way lieth Zarathustra s cave . <end>
<unchecked> <start> My cave is large and deep and hath many corners . <end>
<unchecked> <start> There the best hidden one finder a hiding place . <end>
<unchecked> <start> And close unto it are an hundred things to slip under and creep past , for creeping , fluttering and leaping animals . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Thou outcast who fastest thyself out , thou wilt not stay among men and human pity ? <end>
<unchecked> <start> Up , act like me ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Thus thou earnest even from me . <end>
<unchecked> <start> The doer alone learned . <end>
<unchecked> <start> And speak first , and first of all , with mine animals ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> The proudest animal and the wisest animal they might be the proper counsellors for us both ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Thus spake Zarathustra and went his way , still more thoughtful and slow than before . <end>
<unchecked> <start> For he asked himself many things , and did not easily know the answer . <end>
<unchecked> <start> How poor is man after all ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> he thought in his heart . <end>

<unchecked> <start> Alas , how great must that self love be ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> How much contempt hath it opposed unto it ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Even that man there loved himself even as he despised himself . <end>
<unchecked> <start> A great lover is he , , and a great despised . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Never yet have I found any one who did despise himself more deeply . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Even that is height . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Alas ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> can he have been the higher man whose cry I heard ? <end>
<unchecked> <start> I love the great despises . <end>
<unchecked> <start> But man is a something that must be surpassed . <end>
<unchecked> <start> THE VOLUNTARY BEGGAR When Zarathustra had left the ugliest man , he felt cold and he felt lonely . <end>
<unchecked> <start> For many cold and lonely things passed through his mind , chilling even his limbs . <end>
<unchecked> <start> But when walking on and on , upwards , downwards , now passing green meadows , then over wild stony strata where once an impatient brook had lain down to sleep , he felt all at once warmer and hearties again . <end>
<unchecked> <start> What hath happened unto me ? <end>
<unchecked> <start> he asked himself . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Something warm and living refreshed me . <end>
<unchecked> <start> It must be nigh unto me . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Already I am less alone . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Unconscious companions and brethren hover round me their warm breath touched my soul . <end>
<unchecked> <start> But when he looked round him , and searched for the comforters of his loneliness , behold , there were cows standing on a hill together . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Their nearness and smell had warmed his heart But these cows seemed to listen eagerly unto a speaker , and took no notice of him who approached them . <end>
<unchecked> <start> But when Zarathustra was quite nigh unto them , he heard distinctly a human voice out of the midst of the cows . <end>
<unchecked> <start> And apparently all of them had turned their heads unto the speaker . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Then Zarathustra eagerly hurried up and pushed the animals aside . <end>
<unchecked> <start> For he feared that unto some one harm had been done , which could scarcely be cured by the pity of cows . <end>
<unchecked> <start> But therein he erred . <end>
<unchecked> <start> For behold , there sat a man on the ground , and seemed to persuade the animals not to be shy of him , a peaceful man and mount preacher , out of whose eyes kindness itself preached . <end>
<unchecked> <start> What seeks thou here ? <end>
<unchecked> <start> exclaimed Zarathustra astonished . <end>
<unchecked> <start> What I seek here ? <end>
<unchecked> <start> the man answered . <end>
<unchecked> <start> The same thing as thou seeks , thou disturbed ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> happiness on earth ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> For that purpose I would fain learn from these cows . <end>
<unchecked> <start> For dost thou know ? <end>
<unchecked> <start> Already half the morning I have been addressing them and now they were on the point of giving me their answer . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Why disturbed thou them ? <end>
<unchecked> <start> If we do not turn and become like the cows , we shah not enter into the kingdom of heaven . <end>
<unchecked> <start> For we should learn from them one thing : to ruminate . <end>
<unchecked> <start> And , verily , if man were to gain the whole world and would not learn the one thing , to ruminate of what good would it be ? <end>
<unchecked> <start> He would not get rid of his affliction ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Of his great affliction . <end>
<unchecked> <start> But that to day is called loathing ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Whose heart , mouth and eyes are not filled to day with loathing ? <end>
<unchecked> <start> Thou also ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Thou also ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> But behold these cows ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Thus spake the mount preacher , and then turned his own look unto Zarathustra . <end>
<unchecked> <start> For until then it had clung lovingly unto the cows . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Then he suddenly changed . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Unto whom do I speak ? <end>
<unchecked> <start> he exclaimed , terrified , and leaped up from the ground . <end>
<unchecked> <start> This is the man without loathing , this is Zarathustra himself , the overcome of the great loathing . <end>
<unchecked> <start> This is the eye , this is the mouth , this is the heart of Zarathustra himself . <end>
<unchecked> <start> And speaking thus , he kissed the hands of him unto whom he spake , with his eyes overflowing , and behaved like unto one for whom a valuable gift and treasure hath fallen from heaven unawares . <end>
<unchecked> <start> But the cows gazed at all that and wondered . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Speak not of me , thou strange one , sweet one ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> said Zarathustra , restraining his affection . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Speak first of thyself ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Art thou not the voluntary beggar who once threw away vast riches , Who was ashamed of his riches and of the rich , and fled unto the poorest in order to give them his abundance and his heart ? <end>
<unchecked> <start> But they accepted him not . <end>
<unchecked> <start> But they accept him not , said the voluntary beggar , thou knows it , I see . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Thus at last I have come unto the animals and unto these cows . <end>
<unchecked> <start> There thou learned , said Zarathustra interrupting the speaker , how much harder it is to give properly than to take properly , and that to give well is an art and the last and master art of kindness . <end>
<unchecked> <start> In particular , nowadays , answered the voluntary beggar , i . <end>
<unchecked> <start> e . <end>
<unchecked> <start> to day , when all that is low hath become rebellious and shy and high minded in its own way , i . <end>
<unchecked> <start> e . <end>
<unchecked> <start> in the way of the mob . <end>
<unchecked> <start> For the hour hath come , thou knows it , for the great , bad , long , slow rebellion of the mob and the slaves . <end>
<unchecked> <start> It growth and growth ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Now all alms giving and petty giving make the low rebellious . <end>
<unchecked> <start> And the over rich ought to be on their guard ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Whoever to day letter drops fall , as doth a bottle , out of an all too narrow neck the neck of such a bottle is gladly broken to day . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Voluptuous greediness , bilious envy , angry revenge , pride of the mob , all these things leaped into my face . <end>
<unchecked> <start> It is no longer true that the poor are blessed . <end>
<unchecked> <start> But the kingdom of heaven is with the cows . <end>
<unchecked> <start> And why is it not with the rich ? <end>
<unchecked> <start> asked Zarathustra tempting , while keeping back the cows , which familiarly sniffed at the peaceful one . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Why dost thou tempt me ? <end>
<unchecked> <start> answered he . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Thou knows it thyself still better than I do . <end>
<unchecked> <start> What drove me unto the poorest , O Zarathustra ? <end>
<unchecked> <start> Was it not my loathing of our richest ones ? <end>
<unchecked> <start> Of the convicts guilty of riches , who collect their profit out of all rubbish heaps , with cool eyes and voluptuous thoughts of that rabble that stinker unto heaven , Of that gilded over , falsified mob , whose fathers were thieves or birds of carrion , or rag gatherers with wives complainant , voluptuous , and forgetful for none of them hath a far way to go to become a whore Mob at the top , mob below ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> What are to day poor and rich ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> This distinction have I unlearn . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Then I fled away , further , ever further , until I came unto these cows . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Thus spake the peaceful one , and snuffed himself , and over his words , so that the cows wondered again . <end>
<unchecked> <start> But Zarathustra , all the time the man was speaking so bitterly , gazed with a smile into his face , and silently shook his head . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Thou dost violence unto thyself , thou , in using such bitter words . <end>
<unchecked> <start> For such bitterness neither thy mouth nor thine eye was made . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Nor , , even thy stomach . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Unto it all such anger and hatred and overflowing are repugnant . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Thy stomach desire gentler things . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Thou art not a butcher . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Thou rather seemed unto me to be an eater of plants and roots . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Perhaps thou grandest corn . <end>
<unchecked> <start> But certainly thou art averse from the pleasures of the flesh and thou lowest honey . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Thou hast well found me out , answered the voluntary beggar with his heart lightened . <end>
<unchecked> <start> I love honey , I also grind corn , for I sought what taste sweetly and maketh the breath pure . <end>
<unchecked> <start> I sought also what needed a long time , namely a day s work and a mouth s work for gentle idlers and sluggard . <end>
<unchecked> <start> The highest point , it is true , hath been reached by these cows . <end>
<unchecked> <start> They invented ruminating and lying in the sunshine . <end>
<unchecked> <start> They also abstain from all heavy thoughts that cause flatulence in the heart . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Go to ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> said Zarathustra . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Thou shoulds see mine animals as well , mine eagle and my serpent . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Their like doth not exist on earth this day . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Behold , in this direction leader the way unto my cave . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Be this night its guest ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> And speak with mine animals of the happiness of animals , Until I return home myself . <end>
<unchecked> <start> For now a cry for help called me away from thee in haste . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Thou also wilt find fresh honey with me , golden honey with comb , as cold as ice . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Eat it . <end>
<unchecked> <start> But now take swift farewell of thy cows , thou strange one , thou sweet one ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> although it may be hard unto thee . <end>
<unchecked> <start> For they are thy dearest friends and teachers ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> One excepted whom I love still more , answered the voluntary beggar . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Thou art thyself good , and better even than a cow , O Zarathustra ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Away , away with thee , thou evil flatterer ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> cried Zarathustra mischievously . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Why dost thou spoil me with such praise and honey of flattery ? <end>
<unchecked> <start> Away , away from me ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> he cried once more , and swung his stick after the affectionate beggar , who ran hastily away . <end>
<unchecked> <start> THE SHADOW When the voluntary beggar had hasted away , and Zarathustra was again alone with himself , behind him he heard a new voice crying : Halt ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Zarathustra ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Wait ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Wait ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> It is I , O Zarathustra , I , thy shadow ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> But Zarathustra waited not for a sudden annoyance seized him because of the great crowding and thronging in his mountains . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Whither hath my loneliness gone ? <end>
<unchecked> <start> he said . <end>
<unchecked> <start> This , verily , is becoming too much for me . <end>
<unchecked> <start> These mountains are overcrowded my kingdom is no longer of this world I need new mountains . <end>
<unchecked> <start> My shadow called me ? <end>
<unchecked> <start> What matter for my shadow ? <end>
<unchecked> <start> Let it run after me ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> I run away from it . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Thus spake Zarathustra unto his heart , and ran away . <end>
<unchecked> <start> But he who was behind him , followed him , so that very soon three runners were on the way , one behind the other . <end>
<unchecked> <start> For in the front was the voluntary beggar , then followed Zarathustra , and the third and last was his shadow . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Not long had they run , until Zarathustra came out of his folly and back unto reason , and of a sudden he shook off all annoyance and disgust . <end>
<unchecked> <start> What ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> said he , Have not at all times the most ridiculous things happened unto us old hermits and saints ? <end>
<unchecked> <start> Verily , my folly hath grown high in the mountains ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Now I hear rattle behind each other six legs of old fools ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> But is it allowed unto Zarathustra to be afraid of his shadow ? <end>
<unchecked> <start> Besides , in the long run it hath longer legs than I . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Thus spake Zarathustra , laughing with eyes and intestines . <end>
<unchecked> <start> He stopped and turned quickly round . <end>
<unchecked> <start> And , behold , in so doing he almost threw his follower and shadow unto the ground . <end>
<unchecked> <start> So close did the latter follow at his heels , and so weak was he . <end>
<unchecked> <start> When he looked intently upon him , Zarathustra was terrified as by a sudden ghost . <end>
<unchecked> <start> So thin , black , hollow and workout looked that follower . <end>
<unchecked> <start> What art thou ? <end>
<unchecked> <start> asked Zarathustra violently , What dost thou here ? <end>
<unchecked> <start> And why tallest thou thyself my shadow ? <end>
<unchecked> <start> Thou pleases me not . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Forgive me , answered the shadow , that it is I . <end>
<unchecked> <start> And if I please thee not well , O Zarathustra , in that respect I praise thee and thy good taste . <end>
<unchecked> <start> A wanderer am I who hath already gone far at thy heels ever on the way , but without a goal and without a home , so that , verily , I fall little short of being the eternal , wandering Jew , except that I am neither eternal nor a Jew . <end>
<unchecked> <start> What ? <end>
<unchecked> <start> Must I be ever on the way ? <end>
<unchecked> <start> Whirled about by every wind , unstable , driven away ? <end>
<unchecked> <start> O earth , thou hast grown too round for me ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> On every surface I have sat . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Like the wearied dust , I have fallen asleep on looking glasses and window panes . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Everything taketh from me , nothing giveth I become thin , I am almost like a shadow . <end>
<unchecked> <start> But after thee , O Zarathustra , I have flown and travelled longest . <end>
<unchecked> <start> And though I hid myself from thee , yet have I been thy best shadow . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Wherever thou hast sat , there sat I . <end>
<unchecked> <start> With thee I have haunted the remotest , coldest worlds , like a ghost that voluntarily walked over wintry roofs and snow . <end>
<unchecked> <start> With thee have I striven for everything forbidden , the worst and remotest . <end>
<unchecked> <start> And if anything in me is virtue , it is that I had no fear in the presence of any prohibition . <end>
<unchecked> <start> With thee have I broken whatever my heart revered all landmarks and images I threw down I pursued the most dangerous wishes . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Verily , I have traversed every crime once . <end>
<unchecked> <start> With thee I unlearned the belief in words and values and great names . <end>
<unchecked> <start> When the devil caste his skin , doth not his name fall off as well ? <end>
<unchecked> <start> For that is also skin . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Perhaps the devil himself is skin . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Nothing is true , everything is lawful thus I spake unto myself . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Into the coldest waters I threw myself with head and heart . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Oh , how often have I stood naked , red like a crab through so doing ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Alas , whither hath gone all that is good , and all shame , and all belief in the good ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Alas , whither hath gone that deceitful innocence I once possessed , the innocence of the good and of their noble falsehoods ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Too often , verily , I followed truth close on its heel . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Then it kicked me on the forehead . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Sometimes I thought I lied , and behold ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Only then did I hit upon truth ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Too many things were made clear unto me . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Now it concerned me no more . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Nothing of what I love giveth any longer , why should I love myself still ? <end>
<unchecked> <start> To live , as I like , or to live not at all , thus I will , thus even the holiest one willett . <end>
<unchecked> <start> But alas ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> how do still like ? <end>
<unchecked> <start> Have still a goal ? <end>
<unchecked> <start> A harbour for which my sail is trimmed ? <end>
<unchecked> <start> A good wind ? <end>
<unchecked> <start> Alas , only he who knower whither he sailed , knower also what wind is good , and what is his fair wind . <end>
<unchecked> <start> What is left unto me ? <end>
<unchecked> <start> A heart weary and insolent an unstable will fluttering wings a broken back bone . <end>
<unchecked> <start> This seeking after my home , O Zarathustra , knows thou ? <end>
<unchecked> <start> this seeking was my punishment , it earth me up . <end>
<unchecked> <start> have sought . <end>
<unchecked> <start> I have found it not . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Oh , eternal Everywhere ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Oh , eternal Nowhere ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Oh , eternal In vain ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Thus spake the shadow , and Zarathustra s face grew longer when he heard his words . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Thou art my shadow ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> he said sadly at last . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Thy danger is not small , thou free spirit and wanderer ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Thou hast had a bad day . <end>
<unchecked> <start> See unto it , that a worse evening be not added . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Unto such unstable ones , as thou art , at last even a prison seemeth bliss . <end>
<unchecked> <start> safest thou ever how captured criminals sleep ? <end>
<unchecked> <start> They sleep quietly they enjoy their new security . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Beware lest at last a narrow creed catch thee , a hard , severe illusion ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> For thou art now seduced and tempted by everything narrow and firm . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Thou hast lost thy goal . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Alas ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> how wilt thou bear and brook that loss ? <end>
<unchecked> <start> By it thou hast also lost the way ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Thou poor wandering one , thou fleeting one , thou weary butterfly ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Wilt thou have this night a place of rest and home ? <end>
<unchecked> <start> If so , go up unto my cave ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Yonder goethe the way unto my cave . <end>
<unchecked> <start> And now I will quickly run away from thee . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Already something lieth on me like a shadow . <end>
<unchecked> <start> I will run alone , so that it may again grow light around me . <end>
<unchecked> <start> For that purpose I must be yet a long while gaily on my legs . <end>
<unchecked> <start> But in the evening at my home there will be a dance ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> And Zarathustra ran , and still ran , finding no one else , and was alone ever finding himself again . <end>
<unchecked> <start> And he enjoyed and sipped his loneliness , thinking of good things , through many hours . <end>
<unchecked> <start> But about the hour of noon , when the sun stood exactly over Zarathustra s head , he passed by an old crooked and baggy tree which was embraced round about by the rich love of a vine plant and hidden from itself . <end>
<unchecked> <start> From it an abundance of yellow grapes hung down , offering themselves unto the wanderer . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Then he felt a desire to quench a little thirst and to break off a grape . <end>
<unchecked> <start> When he had stretched out his arm for it , he felt a still stronger desire for something else , to lie down beside the tree , about the hour of perfect noon , and to sleep . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Zarathustra did so . <end>
<unchecked> <start> And no sooner did he lie down on the ground , in the stillness and secrecy of the many coloured grass , than he forgot his little thirst and fell asleep . <end>
<unchecked> <start> For , as Zarathustra s saying hath it : One thing is more necessary than the other . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Only his eyes remained open . <end>
<unchecked> <start> For they could not satisfy themselves with looking at the tree , and at the love of the vine plant , and in praising them . <end>
<unchecked> <start> But when falling asleep , Zarathustra spake thus unto his heart : Hush ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Hush ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Hath the world not this moment become perfect ? <end>
<unchecked> <start> Oh , what happened unto me ? <end>
<unchecked> <start> As a neat wind unseen dance on the panelled sea , light , light as a feather , thus dance sleep on me . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Nor doth it shut mine eye it leave my soul awake . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Light it is , verily , as light as a feather . <end>
<unchecked> <start> It persuade me , I know not how . <end>
<unchecked> <start> It touched me from the inside with a flattering hand . <end>
<unchecked> <start> It compelled me . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Yea , compelled me , so that my soul stretched itself out . <end>
<unchecked> <start> How long and weary it growth unto me , my strange soul ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Did the evening of a seventh day come unto it just at noon ? <end>
<unchecked> <start> Hath it already walked too long happy among good and ripe things ? <end>
<unchecked> <start> It stretched itself out , long , long , longer ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> It lieth still , my strange soul . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Too many good things it hath tasted before . <end>
<unchecked> <start> This golden sadness pressed upon it it maketh a wry mouth . <end>

<unchecked> <start> Is not the land more faithful ? <end>
<unchecked> <start> As such a ship putter to the shore and goethe close in then it is enough that a spider spin its thread unto it from the land . <end>

<unchecked> <start> O happiness ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> O happiness ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Wilt thou sing , O my soul ? <end>
<unchecked> <start> Thou list in the grass . <end>
<unchecked> <start> But this is the secret , solemn hour , when no herdsman played on his flute . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Keep off ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Hot noon sleeper on the fields . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Sing not ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Hush ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> The world is perfect . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Sing not , thou grass bird , O my soul ! <end>

<unchecked> <start> Thus laughed a God . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Hush ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> For happiness how little is required for happiness ! <end>

<unchecked> <start> But it was a blasphemy . <end>
<unchecked> <start> I have now learnt that . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Wise fools speak better . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Just what is least , gentlest , lightest , the rustling of a lizard , a breath , a moment , a twinkling of the eye little maketh the quality of the best happiness . <end>

<unchecked> <start> What hath befallen me ? <end>

<unchecked> <start> Up ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> he said unto himself , thou sleeper ! <end>

<unchecked> <start> Up ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Up ! <end>

<unchecked> <start> Up ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Up ! <end>

<unchecked> <start> Hush ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Hath not the world become perfect this moment ? <end>
<unchecked> <start> Oh , for the golden , round ball ! <end>

<unchecked> <start> And behold ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> there the sun still stood exactly above his head . <end>
<unchecked> <start> And from that , some one might duly suppose that Zarathustra had not slept long . <end>
<unchecked> <start> SALUTATION Late in the afternoon it was when Zarathustra after having searched and strayed about for a long time in vain , returned unto his cave . <end>
<unchecked> <start> But when he stood over against unto it , no longer twenty steps distant from it , that thing came to pass which he expected least . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Anew he heard the great cry for help . <end>
<unchecked> <start> And , astounding ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> this time it came from his own cave . <end>
<unchecked> <start> And it was a long , manifold , strange cry . <end>
<unchecked> <start> And Zarathustra distinguished clearly that it was composed of many voices , though , when heard from a distance , it might sound like a cry from a single mouth . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Then Zarathustra hasted unto his cave , and behold , what spectacle awaited him there after that concert ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> For there they all sat together whom he had passed by during the day : the king on the right and the king on the left the old wizard the pope the voluntary beggar the shadow the conscientious one of the spirit the sad fortune teller and the ass . <end>
<unchecked> <start> And the ugliest man had put a crown on his head , and tied round himself two purple belts . <end>
<unchecked> <start> For , like all ugly folk , he liked to disguise himself and play the gallant . <end>
<unchecked> <start> But in the midst of that sad company stood Zarathustra s eagle , its feathers ruffled and itself disquiet . <end>
<unchecked> <start> For it had been asked to answer many questions for which its pride knew no answer . <end>
<unchecked> <start> And the wise serpent hung round its neck . <end>
<unchecked> <start> At all this Zarathustra looked with great astonishment . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Then he examined each of his guests with gracious curiosity , read the contents of their souls and was once more astonished . <end>
<unchecked> <start> In the meantime they who had gathered there , had arisen from their seats and waited with reverence till Zarathustra should speak . <end>
<unchecked> <start> And Zarathustra spake thus : Ye despairing ones ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Ye strange ones ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Then it was your cry for help I heard ? <end>
<unchecked> <start> And now also know where he is to be sought whom I this day sought for in vain : the higher man . <end>
<unchecked> <start> In mine own cave sitter he , the higher man ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> But why am I astonished ? <end>
<unchecked> <start> Have not I myself alluded him unto myself , by honey offerings , and cunning , enticing calls of my happiness ? <end>
<unchecked> <start> But , ye are not very suitable to form a company , ye make each other s hearts angry , ye criers for help , when sitting together here ? <end>
<unchecked> <start> One must first come One who will make you laugh again , a good , gay clown , a dancer and a wind and romp , some old fool . <end>
<unchecked> <start> What think ye ? <end>
<unchecked> <start> Forgive me , ye despairing ones , that in your presence I speak with such small words , unworthy , verily , of such guests ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> But ye find not out what maketh my heart wanton . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Ye yourselves do so , and your look , forgive me ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> For every one become brave who looked at a despairing one . <end>
<unchecked> <start> To encourage a despairing one for that every one thinkers himself strong enough . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Unto myself ye have given this power , a good gift , my lofty guests ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> An honest guest s gift ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Well then , be not angry at me now offering you something of what is mine also . <end>
<unchecked> <start> This here is my kingdom and my dominion . <end>
<unchecked> <start> But whatever is mine shall be yours for this evening and this night . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Mine animals shah serve you . <end>
<unchecked> <start> My cave shall be your resting place ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> In mine own home and house no one shah despair . <end>
<unchecked> <start> In my province I protect everyone from his own wild beasts . <end>
<unchecked> <start> And this is the first thing I offer you : security ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> But the second thing is my little finger . <end>
<unchecked> <start> And if ye once have it , take the whole hand in addition , yea , and the heart with it ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Welcome here , welcome , my guests and friends ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Thus spake Zarathustra , laughing with love and wickedness . <end>
<unchecked> <start> After this salutation his guests bowed again and were silent in reverence . <end>
<unchecked> <start> And the king on the right answered him in their name . <end>
<unchecked> <start> From the way , O Zarathustra , that thou offered us thy hand and greeting , we know thee to be Zarathustra . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Thou didst humble thyself in our presence . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Thou didst almost wound our reverence for thee . <end>
<unchecked> <start> But who could , like thee , humble himself with such pride ? <end>
<unchecked> <start> That uplifted even us a refreshment is it unto our eyes and hearts . <end>
<unchecked> <start> To behold this alone , we would gladly ascend higher mounts than this mount is . <end>
<unchecked> <start> For we have come as eager sight seers , we longed to see what maketh dim eyes bright . <end>
<unchecked> <start> And behold , all our crying for help is past . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Our sense and heart stand open and are enraptured . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Little is lacking for our courage to become wanton . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Nothing more agreeable , O Zarathustra , growth on earth than a high , strong will . <end>
<unchecked> <start> It is the most beautiful product of earth . <end>
<unchecked> <start> A whole landscape is refreshed by one tree like that . <end>
<unchecked> <start> With the pine , O Zarathustra , I compare him who growth up like thee : tall , silent , hard , alone , of the best and most flexible wood , magnificent And who at last grasped with strong , green boughs after his own dominion , asking strong questions in presence of winds and thunderstorms , and whatever is at home on heights And who giveth stronger answers , a commander , a victorious one ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Oh ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> who would not ascend high mounts in order to see such products ? <end>
<unchecked> <start> In thy tree , O Zarathustra , even the gloomy one , the ill constituted one , rejoice at sight of thee even the restless one become sure and health his heart . <end>
<unchecked> <start> And , verily , unto thy mount and thy tree this day many eyes direct themselves a great longing hath arisen , and many folk learned to ask : Who is Zarathustra ? <end>
<unchecked> <start> And they into whose ear thou hast ever dropped thy song and thy honey , all the hidden , the hermits , and hermits in pairs , spake all at once unto their hearts thus : giveth Zarathustra still ? <end>
<unchecked> <start> It is no longer worth while to live . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Everything is equal , everything is in vain . <end>
<unchecked> <start> If that is to be not so , we must live with Zarathustra ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Why cometh not he who hath announced himself so long ? <end>
<unchecked> <start> thus many ask . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Did loneliness devour him ? <end>
<unchecked> <start> Or we meant to come unto him ? <end>
<unchecked> <start> Now it cometh to pass that loneliness itself warmth mellow and breath like a grave , which breath and can no longer keep its dead . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Everywhere one teeth risen ones . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Now rise and rise the waves around thy mount , O Zarathustra ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> And however high be thy height , many must ascend unto thee . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Thy boat shall not long sit on the dry ground ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> And that we despaired have now come into thy cave , and already despair no more it is merely a sign and omen that better ones are on the way unto thee . <end>
<unchecked> <start> For itself is on the way unto thee , the last relic of God among men , i . <end>
<unchecked> <start> e . <end>
<unchecked> <start> all the men of the great longing , of the great loathing , of the great satietyAll those who do not wish to live , unless they learn to hope again unless they learn from thee , O Zarathustra , the great hope ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Thus spake the king on the right , and seized Zarathustra s hand in order to kiss it . <end>
<unchecked> <start> But Zarathustra hindered his doing reverence and stepped back terrified , as silent and suddenly as though he fled into far distances . <end>
<unchecked> <start> But in a little while he was once more with his guests , gazed at them with bright questioning eyes , and said : My guests , ye higher men , I will speak in German and clearly unto you . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Not for you have I waited here in these mounts . <end>
<unchecked> <start> In German and clearly ? <end>
<unchecked> <start> God a mercy ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> said then the king on the left , secretly . <end>
<unchecked> <start> One teeth that he knower not the dear Germans , this sage from the East ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> But he meant in German and coarsely . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Well ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> that is nowadays not quite the worst taste ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Verily , all of you may be higher men , continued Zarathustra . <end>
<unchecked> <start> But for me , ye are not high and strong enough . <end>
<unchecked> <start> For me , that is to say , for the inexorable which is now silent in me , but will not always be silent . <end>
<unchecked> <start> And if ye belong unto me , ye do so not as my right arm doth . <end>
<unchecked> <start> For whoever standish himself on sick and weak legs , like you , wished above all whether he knower it or hide it from himself to be spared . <end>
<unchecked> <start> But mine arms and my legs I spare not , my warriors I spare not . <end>
<unchecked> <start> How could ye be fit for my warfare ? <end>
<unchecked> <start> By you I should spoil every victory of mine . <end>
<unchecked> <start> And many a one of you would fall unto the ground on hearing the loud noise of my drums . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Besides ye are not beautiful and well born enough for me . <end>
<unchecked> <start> I need pure , smooth mirrors for my doctrines . <end>
<unchecked> <start> On your surface even mine own image is distorted . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Your shoulders are pressed by many a burden , many a memory . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Many an evil dwarf squatted in your corners . <end>
<unchecked> <start> There is hidden mob within even you . <end>
<unchecked> <start> And though ye be high and of a higher tribe , many things in you are crooked and misshapen . <end>
<unchecked> <start> There is no blacksmith in the world to hammer you into shape and straightness . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Ye are only bridges . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Would that higher ones would stride over you unto the other side ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Ye signify stairs . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Then be not angry with him who rise above you unto his own height ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> From your seed one day there may spring unto me a genuine son and perfect heir . <end>
<unchecked> <start> But that is remote . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Ye yourselves are not those unto whom belong mine headship and name . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Not for you wait I in these mounts not with you am I allowed to step down for the last tune . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Ye have come unto me merely as omens , that higher ones are on the way unto me . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Not the men of the great longing , of the great loathing , of the great satiety , and what you called the relic of God . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Nay ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Nay ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Three times Nay ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> For others I wait here in these mounts , and will not lift my feet to depart without them . <end>
<unchecked> <start> I wait for higher ones , stronger ones , more victorious ones , more cheerful ones , such as are built square in body and soul . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Laughing lions must come ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> O my friends and guests , ye strange ones ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Heard ye nothing of my children ? <end>
<unchecked> <start> And that they are on the way unto me ? <end>
<unchecked> <start> Speak , speak of my gardens , of my blissful islands , of my new beautiful kin . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Why speak ye not of them unto me ? <end>
<unchecked> <start> This guest gift I request from your love , that ye speak of my children . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Therefore am I rich , therefore become I poor . <end>
<unchecked> <start> What have I not given away ? <end>
<unchecked> <start> What would I not give away , in order to have one thing : these children , this living plantation , these trees of life of my will and of my highest hope ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Thus spake Zarathustra and suddenly stopped in his speech . <end>
<unchecked> <start> For he was seized by his longing , and he closed his eyes and mouth against the movement of his heart . <end>
<unchecked> <start> And all his guests were silent also and stood still and confounded . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Only the old fortune teller made signs with hands , and gestures . <end>
<unchecked> <start> THE SUPPER For at that point the fortune teller interrupted the salutation between Zarathustra and his guests . <end>
<unchecked> <start> He pressed forward like one who hath no time to lose , seized Zarathustra s hand , and cried : But , Zarathustra ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> One thing is more necessary than another : thus thou thyself safest . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Go to ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> One thing is now more necessary for me than any other . <end>
<unchecked> <start> A word at the proper time : didst thou not invite me to a meal ? <end>
<unchecked> <start> And here are many who have made long journeys . <end>
<unchecked> <start> I suppose thou meanest not to feed us with speeches merely ? <end>
<unchecked> <start> Besides all of you have thought far too much for my taste about dying of cold , by drowning , by suffocation , and about other sorts of bodily danger . <end>
<unchecked> <start> But no one thought of my sort of danger , i . <end>
<unchecked> <start> e . <end>
<unchecked> <start> of dying of hunger . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Thus spake the fortune teller . <end>
<unchecked> <start> But when Zarathustra s animals heard these words , they ran away with terror . <end>
<unchecked> <start> For they saw that all they had brought in during the day would not be sufficient to fill even this one fortune teller s stomach . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Including dying from thirst , the fortune teller went on . <end>
<unchecked> <start> And although I hear water gurgle here , like speeches of wisdom , i . <end>
<unchecked> <start> e . <end>
<unchecked> <start> abounding and never tired I want wine ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Not everyone is a born water drinker like Zarathustra . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Neither is water good for weary and withered ones . <end>
<unchecked> <start> For us wine is proper . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Only it giveth us a sudden vigour and health there and then ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Whereupon , when the fortune teller asked for wine , it came to pass that the king on the left , the silent one for once had a chance to speak . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Wine , he said , hath been provided by us , by myself and my brother , the king on the right . <end>
<unchecked> <start> We have enough of wine , a whole ass ful . <end>
<unchecked> <start> So nothing is lacking but bread . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Bread ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> answered Zarathustra laughing . <end>
<unchecked> <start> It is just bread that hermits lack . <end>
<unchecked> <start> But man giveth not by bread alone , but also by the flesh of good lambs , of which I have two . <end>
<unchecked> <start> They shall be killed swiftly and cooked sicily , with sage . <end>
<unchecked> <start> That is my taste . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Neither are roots nor fruits lacking . <end>
<unchecked> <start> There is enough of them even for and epicurus . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Nor are nuts lacking , or other riddles to crack . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Thus in a little while we will have a good meal . <end>
<unchecked> <start> But he who meant to eat with us , must also put his hand unto the work , the kings included . <end>
<unchecked> <start> For in Zarathustra s home even a king may be a cook . <end>
<unchecked> <start> This proposal met the wishes of the hearts of all only that the voluntary beggar was against meat and wine and spices . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Now listen unto this glutton Zarathustra ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> he said jesting . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Doth one go into caves and high mounts to have such meals ? <end>
<unchecked> <start> It is true , I understand now what we were once taught by him : Let petty poverty be praised ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> And why he seemeth to abolish beggars . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Be of good cheer , answered Zarathustra , as I am so . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Be true unto thine own custom , thou excellent man , grind thy corn , drink thy water , praise thine own cookery , if it only make thee gay ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> I am a law only for those who are mine , I am not a law for all . <end>
<unchecked> <start> But whoever belonged unto me , must be of strong bones , and of light feet , Gay for warfare and festivals , no obscurantist , no dreamer , one ready for what is hardest , like unto his festival , healthy and whole . <end>
<unchecked> <start> What is best , belonged unto my folk and myself . <end>
<unchecked> <start> And if we are not given it , we take it , the best food , the purest sky , the strongest thoughts , the most beautiful women ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Thus spake Zarathustra . <end>
<unchecked> <start> But the king on the right answered : Strange ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Have such clever things ever been heard from the mouth of a wise man ? <end>
<unchecked> <start> And , verily , that is the strangest thing in a wise man , if over and above he is clever and not an ass . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Thus spake the king on the right , and wondered . <end>
<unchecked> <start> But the ass spitefully said Hee Haw unto his speech . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Thus began that long meal which is called The Supper in history books . <end>
<unchecked> <start> And during that meal nothing was spoken of but higher man . <end>
<unchecked> <start> OF HIGHER MAN When , for the first time , I went unto men , I committed the hermit folly , the great folly . <end>
<unchecked> <start> I stood in the market place . <end>
<unchecked> <start> And speaking unto all , I spake unto none . <end>
<unchecked> <start> But in the evening , rope dancers were my companions , and corpses and I myself was almost a corpse . <end>
<unchecked> <start> But with the new morning a new truth came unto me . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Then I learned to say : What matter for me market and mob , and mob s noise and the mob s long ears ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Ye higher men , learn this from me . <end>
<unchecked> <start> In the market no one believe in higher men . <end>
<unchecked> <start> And if ye are going to speak there , it is well ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> But the mob blink : We are all equal ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Ye higher men , thus the mob blink there are no higher men we are all equal man is man in the presence of God we are all equal ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> In the presence of God ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> But now that God hath died . <end>
<unchecked> <start> But in the presence of the mob we do not wish to be equal . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Ye higher men , depart from the market ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> In the presence of God ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> But now hath that God died ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Ye higher men , this God hath been your greatest danger . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Only since he hath lain in the grave , ye have arisen . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Now only cometh the great noon , now only higher man become master ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Understood ye this word , O my brethren ? <end>
<unchecked> <start> Ye are terrified . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Do your hearts grow giddy ? <end>
<unchecked> <start> yawned here an abyss for you ? <end>
<unchecked> <start> market unto you here the hell dog ? <end>
<unchecked> <start> Up ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Up ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Ye higher men ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> It is only now that the mount of man s future giveth birth unto anything . <end>
<unchecked> <start> God hath died . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Now we wish beyond man to live . <end>
<unchecked> <start> The most careful ask to day : How is man preserved ? <end>
<unchecked> <start> But Zarathustra asked as the only and first one : How is man surpassed ? <end>
<unchecked> <start> Beyond man is my care with me , he and not man is the first and only thing . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Not the neighbour , not the poorest one , not the greatest sufferer , not the best one . <end>
<unchecked> <start> O my brethren , what I can love in man , is that he is a transition and a destruction . <end>
<unchecked> <start> And even in you there are many things which make me love and hope . <end>
<unchecked> <start> That ye had scorn , ye higher men , that maketh me hope . <end>
<unchecked> <start> For the great corners are the great reveres . <end>
<unchecked> <start> That ye despaired , therein is much to honour . <end>
<unchecked> <start> For ye did not learn how to give yourselves up ye did not learn petty policies . <end>
<unchecked> <start> For to day the petty folk have become master . <end>
<unchecked> <start> They all preach submission and resignation and policy and diligence and regard and the long etcetera of petty virtues . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Whatever is of the women s tribe , whatever descended from the slaves tribe , and especially from the miss mash of the mob these will now become master of all human fate . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Oh , loathing ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> loathing ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> loathing ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> These ask , and ask , and weary not with asking : How doth man preserve himself best , longest and most agreeably ? <end>
<unchecked> <start> Thereby they are the masters of to day . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Surpass these masters of to day , O my brethren , the petty folk . <end>
<unchecked> <start> They are the greatest danger for beyond man ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Surpass , ye higher men , the petty virtues , the petty policies , the grains of sand regards , the swarming of ants , the miserable ease , the happiness of the greatest number ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> And rather despair than give in ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> And , verily , I love you for the very reason that ye know not how to live to day , ye higher men ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> For thus ye live best ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Have ye courage , O my brethren ? <end>
<unchecked> <start> Are ye stout-hearted ? <end>
<unchecked> <start> I do not mean courage in the presence ot witnesses , but the courage of hermits and eagles , on which not even a God looked any more . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Cold souls , mules , blind folk , drunk folk I do not call stout hearted . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Courage hath he who knower fear but subdued fear he who teeth the abyss , but with pride . <end>
<unchecked> <start> He who teeth the abyss , but with an eagle s eyes he who grasped the abyss with an eagle s claws he hath courage . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Man is evil thus all the wisest men said unto me , as a comfort . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Alas , if that be still true to day ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> For what is evil , is man s best power . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Man must become better and more evil , thus teach . <end>
<unchecked> <start> The vilest is necessary for the best of . <end>
<unchecked> <start> It may have been well for that petty folk s preacher to suffer and bear the burden of man s sin . <end>
<unchecked> <start> But I rejoice in the great sin as in my great comfort . <end>
<unchecked> <start> But such things are not said for long ears . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Every word hath not its proper place in every mouth . <end>
<unchecked> <start> These are fine , remote things . <end>
<unchecked> <start> For them sheep s claws must not grasp ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Ye higher men , think ye that I live to make well what ye made badly ? <end>
<unchecked> <start> Or think ye that I meant to pillow you sufferers more comfortably for the future ? <end>
<unchecked> <start> Or to show new and easier footpaths unto you restless , gone astray on roads and mountains ? <end>
<unchecked> <start> Nay ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Nay ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Three times Nay ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Ever more , ever better ones of your tribe shall perish . <end>
<unchecked> <start> For ye shall have ever a worse and harder life . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Only thus Only thus man growth up unto that height where the lightning strike and breath him high enough for the lightning ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Towards few things , towards long things , towards remote things , my mind and my longing turn . <end>
<unchecked> <start> What concern hath your petty , manifold short misery for me ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Ye do not yet suffer enough ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> For ye suffer from yourselves , ye have never yet suffered from man . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Ye would lie , did ye say otherwise ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> None of you suffered from what have suffered . <end>
<unchecked> <start> It is not enough for me , that the lightning cause no more damage . <end>
<unchecked> <start> I do not want to conduct it into the ground . <end>
<unchecked> <start> It shall learn to work for me . <end>
<unchecked> <start> My wisdom hath for long gathered like a cloud it become tiller and darker . <end>
<unchecked> <start> So doth every wisdom that shall one day give birth unto lightnings . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Unto these men of to day I do not seek to be a light , nor to be called a light by them . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Them I will blind . <end>
<unchecked> <start> O lightning of my wisdom ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Gouge their eyes out ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Will nothing beyond your capacity . <end>
<unchecked> <start> There is an evil falsehood in such as will beyond their capacity . <end>
<unchecked> <start> In particular if they will great things ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> For they cause mistrust towards great things , these fine false corners and actors Until at last they grow false to themselves , have squinting eyes , and are a whited worm eagerness , hidden under strong words , under show off virtues , under shining false actions . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Take great care with such , ye higher men ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> For nothing is to day regarded by me as more valuable and rare than honesty . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Is this To day not of the mob ? <end>
<unchecked> <start> But the mob know not what is great , small , straight , and honest . <end>
<unchecked> <start> They are innocently crooked , they always lie . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Have to day a good mistrust , ye higher men , ye courageous ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Ye with open hearts ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> And keep your reasons secret ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> For to day is of the mob . <end>
<unchecked> <start> But what the mob did not learn to believe without reason , who could upset that for them by reason ? <end>
<unchecked> <start> In the market place one convinced by gestures . <end>
<unchecked> <start> But reasons make the mob mistrustful . <end>
<unchecked> <start> And when in that field truth hath once won a victory , ask yourselves with good mistrust : What powerful error hath fought the battle for it ? <end>
<unchecked> <start> Take care also of scholars ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> They hate you . <end>
<unchecked> <start> For they are sterile ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> They have cold , dried out eyes . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Before them every bird lieth unweathered . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Such folk boast that they do not lie . <end>
<unchecked> <start> But impotence to lie is by no means love unto truth . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Take care ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Freedom from fever is by no means perception ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> I do not credit anything from minds chilled through and through . <end>
<unchecked> <start> He who cannon lie , knower not what truth is . <end>
<unchecked> <start> If ye want to rise high , use your own legs ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Do not let yourselves be carried upwards , sit not down on strange backs and heads ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> But thou didst mount a horse ? <end>
<unchecked> <start> Now thou swiftly rides up unto thy goal ? <end>
<unchecked> <start> Up ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> my friend . <end>
<unchecked> <start> But thy lame leg sitter with thee on horseback ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> When thou hast reached thy goal when thou slightest from thy horse exactly on thy height , thou higher man thou wilt stumble ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Ye creators , ye higher men ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> One is pregnant only of one s own child . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Let nothing be said in your presence , be not persuaded by anything ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Who then is your neighbour ? <end>
<unchecked> <start> And even suppose ye act for the neighbour , ye do not create for him ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Unlearn this for , I pray , ye creators ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Your very virtue wanted you to do nothing with for and for the sake of and because . <end>
<unchecked> <start> To protect yourselves from these deceitful little words , ye shall glue up your ear . <end>
<unchecked> <start> That for the neighbour is the virtue merely of the petty folk . <end>
<unchecked> <start> They say : like and like and hand washed hand . <end>
<unchecked> <start> They have neither the right nor the power for your self interest ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> In your self interest , ye creators , is the caution and providence of the child bearing ones ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> What no one hath ever seen with his eyes , the fruit , is protected and spared and nourished with all your love . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Where all your love is , with your child , there also is all your virtue ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Your work , your will is your . <end>
<unchecked> <start> neighbour . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Allow not yourselves to be talked into false values ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Ye creators , ye higher men ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> He who must give birth is ill . <end>
<unchecked> <start> But he who hath given birth is impure . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Ask women ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> One giveth not birth because the giving of birth cause pleasure . <end>
<unchecked> <start> The pain cause hens and poets to cackle . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Ye creators , in you is much impure . <end>
<unchecked> <start> The reason is that ye were compelled to be mothers . <end>
<unchecked> <start> A new child ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Oh , how much new dirt hath with it been born into the world ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Go unto one side ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> He who hath given birth shall wash his soul pure ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Be not virtuous beyond your ability ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> And demand nothing from yourselves contrary unto probability ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Walk in the footsteps in which your fathers virtue hath gone ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> How could ye rise high , if your fathers will rise not with you ? <end>
<unchecked> <start> But he who desire to be a bristling , may see unto it , that he may not become a lasting also ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> And where the vices of your fathers are , therein ye shall not strive to be saints . <end>
<unchecked> <start> He whose fathers liked women and strong wines and wild boars what , if he were to demand chastity of himself ? <end>
<unchecked> <start> It would be a folly ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> It is much , verily , , for such an one , if he be the husband of one , or two , or three women . <end>
<unchecked> <start> And if he would found monasteries and write over their gates : The way unto what is holy , yet I would say : Wherefore ? <end>

<unchecked> <start> THE SONG OF MELANCHOLYWhen making these speeches , Zarathustra stood close unto the entrance of his cave . <end>
<unchecked> <start> But when uttering the last words , he escaped from his guests and fled for a short while into the open air . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Oh , pure odours round me ! <end>

<unchecked> <start> Come nigh , come nigh , mine eagle and my serpent ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Tell me , mine animals . <end>

<unchecked> <start> Oh , pure odours round me ! <end>

<unchecked> <start> In this way they were all three together at peace , and snuffed and drew in the good air together . <end>
<unchecked> <start> For outside the air was better than among the higher men . <end>
<unchecked> <start> But scarce had Zarathustra left his cave , when the old wizard got up , looked round cunningly and said : He is gone out ! <end>

<unchecked> <start> AMONG DAUGHTERS OF THE DESERT Go not away ! <end>

<unchecked> <start> THE SIGN But the morning after that night , Zarathustra jumped up from his couch , girded his loins , and stepped out of his cave , glowing and strong , like a morning sun coming from dark mountains . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Thou great star he said , as he had said once , thou deep eye of happiness , what would be all thy happiness , if thou hadst not those for whom thou shines ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> And if they would remain in their chambers , while thou art awake and comes and gives and distributes , how angry would thy proud shame be at that ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Up ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> They sleep still , these higher men , whilst am awake . <end>
<unchecked> <start> They are not my proper companions ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Not for them wait I here in my mountains . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Unto my work will I go , unto my day . <end>
<unchecked> <start> But they understand not what are the signs of my morning . <end>
<unchecked> <start> My step is for them not a call that awake them from sleep ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> still at my drunken songs . <end>
<unchecked> <start> The ear that for me , the obeying ear , is lacking in their limbs . <end>
<unchecked> <start> This had Zarathustra said unto his heart , when the sun rose . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Then he asking looked upward , for he heard above him the sharp cry of his eagle . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Up ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> he shouted upward , thus it pleaseth me and is due unto me . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Mine animals are awake , for I am awake . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Mine eagle is awake and , like me , honoured the sun . <end>
<unchecked> <start> With an eagle s claws he grasped for the new light . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Ye are my proper animals . <end>
<unchecked> <start> I love you . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Thus spake Zarathustra . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Then it came to pass that he heard of a sudden that he was surrounded by numberless birds that swarmed and fluttered . <end>
<unchecked> <start> But the whizzing of so many wings , and the thronging round his head were so great that he shut his eyes . <end>
<unchecked> <start> And , verily , like a cloud something fell upon him , like a cloud of arrows discharged over a new enemy . <end>
<unchecked> <start> But , behold , here it was a cloud of love , and it hovered over a new friend . <end>
<unchecked> <start> What happened unto me ? <end>
<unchecked> <start> Zarathustra thought in his astonished heart , and slowly sat down on the big stone which lay beside the exit of his cave . <end>
<unchecked> <start> But while he grasped with his hands round himself , and above himself , and below himself , and kept back the tender birds , behold , something still stranger happened unto him . <end>
<unchecked> <start> He unawares laid hold of dense warm shaggy hair . <end>
<unchecked> <start> At the same time a roaring was heard before him , a gentle , long roaring of a lion . <end>
<unchecked> <start> The sign cometh , said Zarathustra , and his heart changed . <end>
<unchecked> <start> And , in truth , when it grew light before him , there lay a yellow powerful animal at his feet , and clung with its head at his knees , and would not leave him , and did thus out of love , and did as a dog doth when he finder his old master again . <end>
<unchecked> <start> But the doves with their love were no less eager than the lion . <end>
<unchecked> <start> And every time when a dove flew quickly across the nose of the lion , the lion shook its head and wondered and laughed . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Whilst all this went on , Zarathustra said but one thing : My children are nigh , my children . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Then he became quite mute . <end>
<unchecked> <start> But his heart was loosened , and from his eyes tears dropped and fell upon his hands . <end>
<unchecked> <start> And he no more took notice of any thing and sat there unmoved , and without keeping the animals back any more . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Then the doves flew to and fro and sat down on his shoulder , and fondled his white hair , and wearied not with tenderness and rejoicing . <end>
<unchecked> <start> But the strong lion always licked the tears which fell down on Zarathustra s hands , and roared and hummed shyly . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Thus did these animals . <end>
<unchecked> <start> This all took a long time or a short time . <end>
<unchecked> <start> For , properly speaking , for such things there is no time on earth . <end>
<unchecked> <start> But in the meantime the higher men had awakened in Zarathustra s cave and arranged them selves into a procession in order to go to meet Zarathustra and to offer him their morning greeting . <end>
<unchecked> <start> For they had found , when they awoke , that he no more dwelt among them . <end>
<unchecked> <start> But when they came unto the door of the cave , and the sound of their steps went before them , the lion , terribly startled , turned all at once away from Zarathustra , and leaped , wildly roaring , towards the cave . <end>
<unchecked> <start> But the higher men , when they heard him roar , all cried out as with one mouth , and fled back and vanished in a moment . <end>
<unchecked> <start> But Zarathustra himself , stunned and strange , rose from his seat , looked round , stood there astonished , asked his heart , remembered , and was alone . <end>
<unchecked> <start> What heard I ? <end>
<unchecked> <start> he at last said slowly . <end>
<unchecked> <start> What happened unto me this moment ? <end>
<unchecked> <start> And immediately his memory came back , and with one look he understood all that had happened between yesterday and to day . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Here is the stone , he said , and stroked his beard . <end>
<unchecked> <start> On it sat lester morning . <end>
<unchecked> <start> And here the fortune teller stepped unto me and here for the first time I heard the cry I heard this moment , the great cry for help . <end>
<unchecked> <start> O ye higher men , of your need it was that lester morning that old fortune teller told me his tale . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Unto your need he tried to seduce me and tempt me . <end>
<unchecked> <start> O Zarathustra , he said unto me , I come to seduce thee unto thy last sin . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Unto my last sin ? <end>
<unchecked> <start> cried Zarathustra , and angrily laughed at his own word . <end>
<unchecked> <start> What hath been reserved for me as my last sin ? <end>
<unchecked> <start> And once more Zarathustra sank into himself and again sat down on the great stone and meditated . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Suddenly he jumped up . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Pity ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Pity for the higher man ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> he cried out , and his face turned into brass . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Up ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> That hath had its time ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> My woe and my pity , what matter ? <end>
<unchecked> <start> Do I seek for happiness ? <end>
<unchecked> <start> I seek for my work ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Up ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> the lion hath come . <end>
<unchecked> <start> My children are nigh . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Zarathustra hath ripened . <end>
<unchecked> <start> Mine hour hath come ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> This is my morning . <end>
<unchecked> <start> My day beginners ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Come up , then , come up , thou great noon ! <end>
<unchecked> <start> Thus spake Zarathustra , and left his cave , glowing and strong , like a morning sun which cometh from dark mountains . <end>
